ANNEX I
Page 8
Land Tenure and Rural Poverty
19.       An overwhelming proportion of all farmers in Mexico are classified
as low-income producers. For example, the 1960 Census revealed that 84% of
all farms reported annual production values of less than US$400 and this 84%
accounted for only 21% of the total value of national agricultural production
value. The private landowners, as a group, are no better off than the
ejidatarios, except for a very small number. There is no reason to expect
that the situation has improved materially since 1960.
20.       Statistics of the Department of Agrarian Affairs and Colonization
(DAAC) prove that as more and more peasants clamor for land, the Government
finds it necessary to create more and more ejidos. According to one official
statistical summary by DAAC, in the 1960-71 period, over 6,600 presidential
resolutions were signed for the creation of ejidos, ejidatario community
centers, or identification of communal lands, covering over 35 million ha of
land and affecting some 693,000 ejidatarios.  Consequently) :- the ejidatario
population increases, the "pequeno propietario" class decreases. Thus, the
land tenure system of Mexico has been converted from one dominated by
"latifundistas" (owners of very large proporties) to one that is now
dominated by "minifundistas".
21.       The low-income producer must have long-term credit for productive
investments, in order to improve his family's welfare.  Unfortunately, as an
individual farmer, he is only rarely eligible for credit, for want of land
and other assets with which to guarantee repavments. When ways and means are
found to offer him credit, through organization of poor, small farmers into
cooperative groups he still has an intense need for technical assistance and
training, in order to make the best possible use of his credit. But the low-
income producer is also usually stymied for want of certain basic or minimum
essential infrasturctural facilities.
22.       The fact that only a half of the available cropland was harvested
in 1970 (para 15) indicates need for more land clearing which, in many
instances can be done most efficiently with heavy equipment. Although
Mexico has a creditable record in the construction of farm-to-market roads
manv communities are still isolated and lacking ready access. Such access
roads are best built as local or state projects, with self-help by the commu-
nity. In a similar vein, many communities suffer for want of electricity
which is especially critical with reference to irrigation from deep wells.
The same sort of deficiencies are noted with reference to a number of other
community facilities (storage warehouses, small consumer goods stores, health
clinics, potable water and so forth). Altogether, the lack of such community
facilities represents a big constraint that, fortunately, is clearly recognized
by the present administration.
23.       The multiplicity of rural problems and constraints are reflected in
rural underemployment, lack of training and lack of opportunity as well as in
the fact that the rural labor force is relatively unproductive. It is quite



ANNEX. I
Page 9
apparant that Mexico's agrarian reform efforts have fallen short of the goals
and while it is true that millions of people have been resettled who otherwise
might have greatly aggravated the serious urban slum situations, it is equally
true that most of the ejidos and most of the small landowner communities must
still be viewed as depressed areas. Obviously, a great need exists for helping
the low-income producers to work together on some sort of a group or coopera-
tive basis but such a movement cannot be successful unless it is coupled with
much greater efforts in training, applied research and teclnical assistance.
C. Government Services
24.       The Government's recent new emphasis on rural welfare and develop-
ment is a hopeful sign. Several public agencies concerned with agriculture
are being given increased responsibilities and budgets and special attention
is being directed to the ejidos and to other groups of low-income producers.
Additionally, the banking systems -- both public and private -- are being en-
couraged to increase greatly their medium and long-term lending for improve-
ments designed to increase productivity and family incomes. The proper coordi-
nation and orientation of these renewed efforts will be of vital importance.
Agrarian Affairs
25.       The DAAC is the agency directly responsible for most aspects of
ejido establishment, supervision and improvement.  According to the General
Secretary of ejido promotion and organization of DAAC (a former FONDO technician),
all of the following measures are required in order to solve the problem of the
poor rural communities:
(a) Organize groups of farmers, especially for the purchase of
inputs and for marketing;
(b) Improve basic infrastructure -- access roads, water facil-
ities, electrification and miscellaneous community facili-
ities;
(c) Greatly increase medium to long-term credit for production
improvements;
(d) Provide much more training of key community leaders or
technicians, and
(e) Establish more small industries and service enterprises.
26.       DAAC is increasing and upgrading its staff from a present 360 to
750 technicians, most of them in the sub-professional (perito) class and a
special institute has been created (but lacks financing) to train ejidatarios
to better manage their own affairs). DAAC is keenly aware of its own limita-
tions and of the great need for well coordinated assistance by a number of
other agencies, including the public and private banks.



ANNEX I
Page 10
27.       The Fondo Nacional de Fomento Ejidal (FONAFE) is closely related to
DAAC and manages funds that have been acquired by ejidos from timber sales,
land expropriations and various other sources. With the approval of ejido
councils FONAFE uses these funds to establish small industries, to help finance
land clearing for housing, to build ejido food stores and for other enterprises.
Agricultural Education, Research and Extension
2(R.      The present Government has demonstrated its appreciation of the
fundamental importance of well coordinated agricultural education, research
and extension services by substantially increasing budgets and upgrading
staffs (Appendix 1-3). The very small percentage of Mexican farms that can
now be classified as "modern" is a clear indication of the great scope of the
job to be done. As described in Appendix 1-3 the FONDO, whose program gives
major attention to the livestock industry, is cognizant of certain weaknesses
in livestock research and is attempting to provide some necessary assistance
through this Fourth Project. This assistance, however, will be small by com-
parison with the urgent need to increase the production of beef, milk and
pork, as well as a number of other commodities (para 22 and Table 1). Applied
research and demonstrations appear to be high priority requirements for achiev-
ing the kind of breakthroughs that will bring the very substantial production
increases needed.
29.       Education.  Agricultural education facilities in Mexico have showni
great improvements in recent years. The Universities at Chapingo and Monter-
rey are well recognized throughout the hemisphere and more post graduate de-
grees are being offered. The schools offering sub-professional degrees, how-
ever, are only beginning to gain recognition but they are important since the
future technical manpower requirements are such that many more sub-professionals
must be emploved in order to free the higher trained personnel so that they can
spend mnore time on technical problems and less time on routine matters.
30.       Research. The structure of research facilities in Mexico appears
to be quite satisfactory, having been developed over the past 25 years with
aid from the Rockefeller Foundation and other agencies. In general, the crop
research is more advanced than livestock research, probably because work with
livestock has been heavily oriented toward disease control rather than manage-
ment. Despite some outstanding exceptions (e.g. wheat) agricultural research
has failed to satisfy the needs of the Sector.  There is an urgent need for a
considerable increase in many aspects of crop and livestock research. The
main focus should be a production oriented research program that provides
sound data on which to base more productive and viable farming operations.
31.       Extension.  At the present time, agricultural extension services are
undergoing a very substantial reorganization and expansion. Besides the con-
ventional type of services supplied by the Federal and State Governments, sim-
ilar assistance is being provided by banks and a number of Government agencies
to selected clients and all are being expanded. With all of this new build-up,
however, it mlust be recognized that the needs for technical assistance by the
ittraditional" and "subsistence" farmers (para 7) are both extremely urgent and,
as yet, largely unsatisfied.



ANNEX I
Page 11
0           Marketing Aids
32.       Price supports are offered by Compania Nacional de Subsistencias
Popularies (CONASUPO), a major federal agency that has become heavily involved
in a number of activities associated with the production, marketing and con-
sumption of basic food items. Thus, CONASUPO is primarily concerned with both
the rural problem of low-income producers and the urban problem of low-income
consumers.
33.       In cooperation with other agencies, CONASUPO is helping ejido admin-
istrative councils to establish and manage "bodegas" and "tiendas". The bode-
gas serve as warehouses for farm inputs (such as fertilizers, seed and farm
equipment) and also as temporary storehouses for grain and other products.
The bodegas are being built with ejidatario labor to a standard design, with
a 500metric ton capacity, and they are finally turned over to ejido manage-
ment. CONASUPO will rent storage space in the bodegas for price supported
crops and the income so derived will enable the ejido to pay for the cost of
construction. During the next four years another 3,000 bodegas will be built,
The tiendas are stores for the provision and sale of basic subsistence items
that are now being distributed and sold by CONASUPO on a nation-wide basis.
They are financed by FONAFE (para 31) and are built (also standardized designI
with the help of INDECO (National Institute for Communitv Development).
CONASUPO's goal is to place 5,000 tiendas in operation by 1976.
34.       In order to assure proper management of bodegas, tiendas and other
ejido facilities, CONASUPO also has taken the lead in organizing and conducting-
special training courses for ejidatorios (mostly young men, 18 to 30 years of
age). Courses range from one to three months and many bodegas are presently
serving as classrooms. It is planned to step up this program to a point where
30,000 persons will be trained each year.
35.       ANDSA (National Warehouse Company) has been the Government's official
institution for the storage of grain and other commodities since 1936. This
agency has 172 warehouses throughout the country, with a total bulk storage
capacitv of 4 million tons (in addition to 3 million tons of private storage
capacity) and is now in a position to handle all kinds of agricultural commo-
dities including those requiring cold storage. With its professional staff
and modern facilities, A^DSA also provides technical assistance to other Gov-
ernment undertakings such as those described above for CnO'AStPO.

APPENDIX 1-1
MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION CHARACTERISTICS
A. Diversity of Production
1.        Because of Mexico's numerous ecological zones, a real potential
exists for great diversity in agricultural production. Along the Gulf Coast,
corn, sorghum, and beef cattle predominate. In the northern part of this
coastal area, cotton was important until recently, but has now been almost
completely replaced by lower risk crops, such as sorghum. Natural pastures
of the dry and wet Ruastecas support a large beef cattle herd, while south,
in Tabasco, beef production is important and cacao, coffee, and bananas are
the major crops.
2.        Similar diversity exists along the Pacific Coast.  The northwestern
region is arid, but some of the largest irrigation projects have been devel-
oped here producing mostly cotton, wheat, vegetable oilseeds and horticul-
tural crops. Farther south, the coastal plain narrows and has a seasonally
wet tropical climate, suitable for tropical fruits as well as for the tradi-
tional basic food crops. In Chiapas, the plain is wider, and corn, beans,
sesame, cotton and beef cattle are important.
3.        The densely populated highlands and central plateau were formerly
known as Mexico's breadbasket but, today, productivity is greater in the low-
lands, mainly due to irrigation development. The arid and semi-arid zones
in the north have good natural pastures, and it is from these areas that the
bulk of the male weaners exported to the US originate. Irrigated areas
around Chihuahua and Torreon produce cotton, wheat, beans, alfalfa and
fruits. An impressive dairy industry based on alfalfa has been developed
in the Comarca Lagunera region around Torreon, and the central altiplano to
the south (states of Jalisco and Michoacan) produces corn, beans, sorghum,
and other food crops for Guadalajara and the FD (Mexico City market). Around
the FD (states of Mexico, Puebla, and Hidalgo), the altiplano is temperate
and dry, producing rainfed corn and beans, while small irrigated areas pro-
duce fruits and horticultural crops. The dairy industry, centered around the
FD, is supplied with alfalfa from this area.
B. Livestock
4.        Approximately 40% of the total area of Mexico, or 78 million ha
is classified as being best suited for livestock production (many beef),
principally on native grasslands. Table 1 shows the trend in livestock num-
bers by decades since 1930 and annually since 1960 for cattle, sheep, goats,
pigs, and poultry.



APPENDIX 1-1
Page 2
Beef Production
5.        Mexico has two major beef cattle production regions:  (a) the arid
and semi-arid northern rangelands and (b) the wet and dry tropics along both
the Gulf and Pacific coasts. The northern region is so situated as to be
geared primarily to the US market. The tropical areas produce exclusively
for the domestic market. The following table indicates the trends in total
cattle production of Mexico:
Item                            1965       1967       1969       1972
1.   Total Cattle Population in '000s  21,975    23,294    24,875    25,600
2.   Domestic Slaughter in '000s           2,420      2,325     2,740      3,320
3.   Meat Product in '000 tons               394        376        495       515
4.   Live Cattle Exports in '000s            557        524        840       770
5.   Total Output in '000s                 2,977      2,849     3,890      4,090
6.   Offtake % 5:1                            13.5       12.2       15.6       16.0
Source: Secretaria of Agriculture.
6.        The arid and semi-arid northern rangeland produces feeder cattle,
mainly for the US market under extensive conditions with stocking rates in
excess of one ha per head. British breeds, particularly Hereford and Aberdeen
Angus, predominate.  Only male weaners are exported since the export of
females is prohibited, although this ban was temporarily lifted in 1969
because of drought. In some of the irrigated northern areas, there is a
very small market for export-type cattle for fattening in feedlots. Some
female weaners are used for this purpose when they can be sold to specialty
markets (higher prices), which makes confinement fattening economical. Cull
cows from the northern rangelands account for half the frozen boneless beef
exports to the United States; the other half originates in the central pla-
teau. Weaning percentage varies from 30% to 50%, depending on management
and disease but mainly on nutrition. In this region, a program of deferred
grazing at proper stocking levels should make it possible to increase weaning
rates significantly. Recent attempts to improve the native rainfed patures
by the introduction of Buffel grass have also been quite successful, particu-
larly in the semi-arid areas with more than 15 inches of rain per year.
7.        The wet and dry tropics produce beef almost exclusively for domes-
tic consumption and support about 60% of Mexico's cattle population. Pro-
duction methods are generally inefficient, pastures are underutilized, and
nutritional deficiencies are widespread. Considering the experience of other
countries with similar environments (notably Australia), there is little doubt
but that, with better animal and pasture management, together with the use of
more technology, production could be greatly increased. The most pressing



APPENDIX 1-1
Page 3
need is for grazing studies on both native and introduced pastures in vari-
ous ecological zones, on stocking rates under various conditions, and on
fertilizer requirements. In this work, particular emphasis should be placed
on the importance of legumes in the pasture system. Many native species of
legumes abound in the area but, to date, tropical legumes have not been used
as components of commercial pasture mixtures. The introduction of these
legumes, plus the application of fertilizers where necessary (principally
phosphate and micro-elements) would greatly increase the longevity and pro-
ductivity from these artificial pastures. Work, however, is needed to demon-
strate the best species and to determine the most economical methods of
establishment and management. Although the FONDO is helping to solve these
problems, further more critical studies are needed.
Dairy Production
8.        Approximately 2.8 million cows of recognized dairy breeds, particu-
larly Holstein-Friesian are concentrated in the irrigated districts of the
central plateau and north. In addition, many cows are milked in the tropi-
cal areas but these are crossbred animals of low productivity. In tropical
areas of Mexico, as in most tropical areas of Latin America, milk and milk
products are by-products of the beef industry and provide ranchers with
needed daily income. Accurate information is not available on Mexican milk
production, but it is estimated as follows:
Number of         Number of              Milk Production
Year             Cows Milked       Goats Milked            Cows        Goats
('000 head)       ('000 head)            (million liters)
1965               2,668             1,121               3,963          161
1968               2,767             1,079               4,274          155
1969               2,878             1,216               4,421          175
1972               3,260             1,300               5.200          178
Source: Secretaria de Agricultura.
9.        Traditionally, dairying has been concentrated principally in the
irrigated districts of the central plateau, and while the Bajio region has
been the most important and traditional milkshed for the FD, nearly 40% of
its supply originates within the FD. Production is generally based on
irrigated and greenfed alfalfa and confinement management. However, in
recent years, intensive dairy production under confinement management has
also been initiated in the irrigated districts of the north and northwest,
particularly around Torreon and the milk from this are is now the principal
single supplier to the FD market. The Torreon dairy coop (producing near a
half million liters per day) also owns pasteurizing plants in both Mexico
City and Acapulco.
.



APPENDIX 1-1
Page 4
10.       Although production levels per cow are very high under this inten-             i
sive system, costs per liter of milk are also high because of the feeding
and capital costs. Under Mexican conditions it should be possible to adopt
lower cost production methods based on grazing herds on permanent grass
legume pastures, such as in New Zealand and Ireland, but studies are needed
to test the validity of this hypothesis.
Pork Production
11.       Commercial pig production has always been concentrated in the
western edge of the Bahio but more recently, emphasis has shifted to the
adjacent northwest irrigated states because of the increasing availability
of grain sorghum there. Approximately 90% of the intensive operations are
combined breeding/fattening under confinement management. The national off-
take rate is approximately 30% but with better management, aimed particularly
at increasing the weaning percentage, this could be improved considerably.
Approximately 85% of the pigs slaughtered in the ED are produced in the
three states of Guanajuato, Michoacan and Jalisco, but northwestern states
are increasing their share. Breeding and production methods are changing to
provide the meat-type animal coming into demand. The remainder of Mexico's
pig production is based largely on the family-type operation whose produc-
tion does not greatly affect the principal FD market.
Poultry and Egg Production
12.       The daily egg production in Mexico is estimated to number about                0
32 million, one-third of which is consumed in the FD and about 360 tons of
broilers are produced daily, a great part of which is marketed in the FD.
The fact that poultry is no longer imported and prices are stable or de-
creasing suggests that the industry grew vigorously in the recent past and
has become stabilized. The principal egg producing areas are located in the
northwest, northcentral, and northeast irrigated zones and in the central
plateau. The typical northern producer has more than 50,000 layers and
practices modern production technology, which has resulted in a reduction
in the costs. Most commercial production of broilers is situated in the
central plateau near Mexico City and in the FD, although such production in
the irrigated northwest is gaining in importance. Poultry numbers, which
remained almost stable until 1965, have increased rapidly since that time.
B. Crops and Irrigation
13.       Crop production under the Fourth Project will be largely concen-
trated on farms that either now are irrigated or that can be improved with
deep wells and related water facilities. Most of the public investments for
agricultural development are still being devoted to irrigation works.
0



APPENDIX 1-1
Page 5
0
This heavy emphasis on water facilities development is supported by the fact
that the present total irrigated area of 4.1 million ha represents only about
one-third of the cropland area that can, potentially, be irrigated. Much of
the 61% of all cropland area that is devoted to corn and beans is located in
the rainfed areas. More intensive extension and applied research efforts are
required, along with long-term investment credit, in order to increase yields
and diversify these traditional farming practices.
14.       Only 7.4% of all farms in Mexico (including ejidataris parcels)
are classified as "modern", 40.1% are classified as "traditional" (i.e.
using traditional methods but producing substantial amounts of cash crops);
while the remaining 52.5% are classified as "subsistence" farms (producing
very little for the cash market). The "modern" farm is larger; income per
hectare is four times that of the "subsistence" farm; and most of the modern
farmers have irrigation, and use fertilizers and improved seed. Most of the
corn and beans are produced by the relatively poor traditional and subsist-
ence farmers, while the modern farmers produce most of the cotton, sorghum,
wheat, sugarcane, fruits, and vegetables.
15.       The Government plans to extend irrigation over an additional area
of 1,000,000 hectares during the next five years and considers that the SRH
(Ministry of Tlydraulic Resources) has the capability of handling such a
load. Highest priority is being given to the extension of the Rio Sinaloa
project and development of the Panuco and Papaluapan Basins. The SRH also
is placing special emphasis on the construction of small-scale irrigation
facilities to benefit groups of ejidatarios or similar communities of low-
income landowners. This so-called "community development" effort is of
particular importance in connection with the Low-Income Producers Sub-Project.
16.       Another SRH program effort is of fundamental importance to this
Project.  This program is called Plamepa (Plan de Mejoramiento Parcelario)
and it aims to reduce waste in the use of water, and to improve and diversify
agricultural practices. Since 1967 the Plamepa program has been expanded to
cover 130,000 hectares but the Government now comtemplates a much more rapid
expansion, with IDB financing. The on-farm use of water is notably defici-
ent in Mexico. The Plamepa program, which entails close cooperation with
the public agricultural credit banks, will have a most important bearing on
the future impact of this Bank Project.
Cotton
17.       Although Mexico's cotton production has recovered somewhat from
a 1970 low of 1.44 million bales the current harvest of 1.71 million bales
is still short of the 2.58 million bale 1965 production. With the prospect
of declining prices during the mext several years and with competition from
other crops, the official projections for cotton (1976 and 1982) indicate
that the 1965 production levels will not be repeated. The causes of this
.



APPENDIX 1-1
Page 6
decline are well documented:  severe cutback of the area planted in rainfed
areas due to declining fertility, rising costs, and the like competition from
wheat, sorghum and vegetable oils which enjoy high support prices (Appendix
1-2); and the almost complete withdrawal from cotton production in the northeast,
which is closeest to ocean shipping ports. The most serious cutback, however,
has come in the rainfed areas, which declined from 40% (in 1965) to less than
16% of the harvested area. Continuous production in these areas has led to
serious pest problems, declining yields and great risks of crop failure. In
fact, in some areas the Government has stopped offering crop insurance, thus
causing farmers to turn to other crops like sorghum, for which insurance and
price supports are available.
18.       In irrigation districts, the cutback in area planted to cotton has
been less marked than in rainfed areas, inspite of the counter attractions
of price-supported cereal and oil seed crops which offer little risk. How-
ever, pest and disease control costs have also increased because of inade-
quate crop rotations, the continuous frowing of small number of varieties,
and inefficient spraying techniques. The cost of fertilizers and pesticides
(including application) now make up roughly 40% of cotton production costs
in many cotton farming areas. Seasonal labor shortages have also contributed
to the decline.
19.       Cotton growing is now concentrated in the most appropriate areas,
although some high-risk cotton will continue to be grown by rainfed farmers,
with unpredictable results.  Intensive investigation is currently underway
though to find ways of boosting cotton production, and FONDO's technical
services and credit facilities are assured of playing a key role in any pro-
gram devised.
Fruits and Vegetables
20.       Mexico shows great promise as an important producer of fruits and
vegetables for export, as well as for the rapidly growing domestic market.
Over 30 fruits are now rated as commercially important and, while tomatoes
are currently the outstanding vegetables a number of others are also gaining
importance. Fruits are being produced by about 50,000 farmers on about
3.3% of the total cultivated area (some 511,000 ha); yet they account for
close to 20% of total crop value. Annual fruit production is valued at more
than Mex $6 million. Although the citrus fruits and bananas now cover half
of the total orchard area, avocado, strawberries, mango, melons, papaya,
guava, pecans, apples and others are of grow-ing importance. The comparative
advantages of fruits over annual crops may be seen from the following data:

The employment aspect of fruit production is of particular importance in
Mexico.  The average orchard requires an estimated 123 man days per ha
versus 50 man days per ha for annual crops.  Additionally, industrialization
of fruit in 1966 involved 370 plants and 30,000 employees.
21.       In 1970, total agricultural exports to the US came to US$513 mil-
lion, of which nearly 40% was for fruits and vegetables. The major export
commodity was tomatoes, due essentially to the fact that Mexico can produce
vine-ripe tomatoes at about one-third the cost of production in Florida.
Appreciable volumes of peppers, cucumbers, onions were also exported and
three important foreign food processors have recently become involved in
asparagus, partly, however, at the expense of strawberry production.
March 23, 1973

MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Markets and Marketing
1.        Mexico's population is presently growing at about 3.4% per annum
and per capita income by about 3.0% per annum. Assuming an income elasticity
of demand of about 0.37, this results in approximately a 4.5% annual increase
in the quantity of food demanded. The expanding domestic market should
readily absorb most of the expected incremental Project production (Table 2).
This is true of beef, milk, eggs, broilers, pork, and tree crops. Most of
the incremental production of feeder cattle and cotton would be exported.
Beef Cattle
2.        The domestic market comprises local markets and the large urban
centers. The Federal District (FD) of Mexico City is the most important,
and, although it has only about 13Z of Mexico's population, approximately
one-third of all meat is consumed there. In attempting to better satisfy
the urban markets at acceptable price levels, the Government has, in the
past, resorted to interfering with the free market mechanism. The inter-
ference has taken two forms:  (a) price controls on beef at the wholesale
0            and retail levels; and (b) requirement that exporters of boneless beef and
feeder cattle send a quota of live cattle, based on the volume of exports,
to the FT and other urban centers. The situation in the FD was further
complicated by the monopoly position of the municipal abattoir (Ferreria) 1/.
Quotas are also applied by the Government to the quantities of boneless
beef and male feeder cattle that can be exported.
3.        An ideal situation in beef marketing would exist in Mexico if the
northern areas were free to export their feeder cattle to the United States
at prevailing lucrative price levels and if the domestic market could be
satisfied from tropical areas. Realization of this situation, however, has
so far been prevented by the inability of tropical areas to satisfy domestic
markets at acceptable price levels.
4.        The trend in marketing is toward the above, or free market, system.
In 1966, the monopoly of the Ferreria in slaughtering cattle for the FD was
broken by the Government, due to the pressure of producers and allegations
of mismanagement. Following this, ranchers in the states of Tabasco and
Veracruz started to slaughter their own cattle and to sell directly to
wholesalers and retailers in the FD, a practice that has prevented the
Ferreria from reasserting a monopoly position. As the supply of beef to
the FD has improved, the Government has relaxed its enforcement of price
controls and quotas, and, when the tropical areas are capable of assuring
1/   The official name of the abattoir is Industrial de Abastos, S.A. but it
G   -    is commonly known as Rastro de Ferreria.



APPENDIX 1-2
Page 2
an adequate beef supply sufficient to satisfy domestic demand at acceptable               0
price levels, Government intends to eliminate them completely. Consequently,
the best way to rationalize the Mexican beef marketing system is to boost
production in the tropical areas. The recent IBRD-supported Livestock and
Agricultural Development Projects had this as a primary objective, and the
proposed Project would continue the effort. Furthermore, the Project would
help the marketing of beef by providing funds and technical services for
slaughterhouses in tropical areas. Elsewhere in the country, main slaughter-
ing facilities appear adequate.
5.        The ban on the export of females is a disincentive to production
in northern areas but its permanent removal will come only when the domestic
market is fully independent of supplies from the northern areas. Generally,
the ban is rigidly enforced, except in drought years like 1969/70 when the
export of 242,000 females was authorized.
6.        Mexico is the third largest exporter of frozen beef to the United
States. In 1971, boneless beef exports to that market amounted to 41,360
metric tons, down from 50,933 metric tons in 1970. On June 26, 1972, the
United States suspended, for the rest of 1972, the quota system on beef
imports and, because of rapidly rising meat prices in the United States,
the President of that country recently suspended quotas on beef imports for
the remainder of 1973. Nevertheless, the uncertainty surrounding the future
of this market means that ranchers and meat processors in Mexico have to
greatly discount any future growth prospects.
7.       ?hexico is the largest exporter of feeder cattle (male weaners) to
the United States and, since the United States imposes no quota, opportunities
for expansion are excellent.
Milk
S.        Mexico imports large quantities of dairy products, particularly
evaporated and non-fat dried milk. The value of imported evaporated and
condensed milk reached Mex$120 million in 1970 and averaged about Mex$100
million for the years 1966-70. Milk is subject to official retail price
controls and the official controlled price for first-class milk in the FD,
Mexico's principal milk market, is Mex$2.20 per liter. In 1964, milk
producers sought and obtained a "temporary" court injunction against selling
at the official prices. This injunction, which is still in effect, has
enabled milk prices in the FD to reach a level close to the free market
level. The current retail price in the Fl) is about Mex$2.60 per liter for
first-class milk and about Mex$2.25 per liter for second class.
9.        Government regulations, originating 20 years ago, stipulate that
first-class milk must be pasteurized on the farms producing it, but in the
past, this led to a proliferation of uneconomic sized plants. Although the
law is still in existence, special exemptions are granted by the Secretaria
de Sanidad, Salubridad y Assistencia, who is charged with administering the
law.
0



APPENDIX 1-2
Page 3
10.       The milk marketing system, despite its limitations, is evolving
toward a rational system, mainly from the pressure of producer organizations
that have entered the field in recent years. Many cities and towns are in
need of good quality, reasonably priced milk, and the Project, by supporting
pasteurization plants, credits and technical services to dairy farm producers,
and research into cheaper production systems, would play a vital role in
helping to provide this essential commodity to more and more Mexicans.
Pork
11.       Pork to supply the FD originates mainly in the Central Plateau,
although production is increasing in the grain producing areas.  National
demand is growing rapidly as is production despite the high feed grain
prices, because producer profit margins are normally good. The present
marketing system is satisfactory for the Project.
Eggs
12.       Production resulting from the Project can easily be handled by the
present system. The Federal Government, however, should carefully study the
barriers to growth and rationalization of the industry arising from the
tendency of states to protect their own producers by impeding the free move-
ment of eggs across state boundaries. The Project would support egg packing
plants required to handle increased production in the years ahead.
Broilers
13.       Until 1969, it was illegal for private processing plants to supply
broilers to the FD. That ban has been lifted and private plants now exist
in the FD as well as in Hermosillo, Monterrey, and Guadalajara. The monopoly
position held until 1969 by the Ferreria in the FD broiler market was a
serious disincentive to increased production, and it led to inefficiencies
in marketing, especially by strengthening the position of middlemen. Even
today, the producer is effectively barred by the middlemen from selling
directly to processors. The inevitable result is high marketing costs from
the producer to the retailer.
14.       Poor presentation of broilers to the consumer is recognized as a
deterrent to market penetration, but, in January 1970, a law was passed to
regulate the quality of broilers offered to the public and to eliminate
abuses.
15.       The industry at the producer level is high cost and fragmented.
Many of the producers lack the technical and business ability to manage a
flock of sufficient numbers to realize all economies of size, but large
integrated production and processing units are beginning to emerge. Such
units would have the market power to transform the system and to survive if
prices decline. The profit margins of these large integrated units are wide
and their ability to offer a standard superior product directly to retail
outlets is appealing, especially to supermarkets.



APPENDIX 1-2
Page 4
16.       The Project would support production units that have good possi-               0
bilities of low cost efficient production. Integrated production and pro-
cessing plants would be preferred because of their greater chances of sur-
vival should a serious price decline occur.
Cotton
17.       Mexican strict middling cotton (1-1/16"') lias always had ready
acceptance on world markets because of good quality.  Most is hand picked
and is grown in arid climates. Major markets are Japan and Europe. The
increased export availability likely to arise from the Project -- about
300,000 bales -- would not even enable Mexico to reach its former export
level of 1.8 million bales in 1965, since it had fallen to 1.0 million by
1970 (Table 3). World cotton prices are projected to decline by approxi-
mately 15% during the 1970's but Mexican cotton exports would still be
competitive (Annex 11).
18.       Mexico's cotton marketing system is efficient.  Ginning charges,
although rising, are low because of the large volume handled per gin, low
cost labor, and low tare.  Storage is not normally needed by handlers because
of the usually dry climate and quick disposal of the cotton crop. Further-
more, keen competition among merchants and low labor charges make it possible
to keep the cost of moving cotton from gin to shipside at a minimum.
Wheat, Corn, Sorghum, Vegetable Oilseeds, and Beans
19.       All these crops have Government-guaranteed minimum prices to the
producer, which assures a market for Project production (Table 4). Presently,
the prime responsibility for these programs rests with the Compania Nacional
de Subsistencias Populares (CONASUPO), in cooperation with agricultural banks
and the Government's grain storage corporation, Almacenes Nacionales de
Deposito, S.A. (ANDSA). ANDSA has increased its capacity from 80,000 to
about 4 million tons in the past 30 years, and there is a private grain
storage capacity of close to 3 million tons. To further help grain market-
ing, the Project would support some on-farm storage facilities, especially
in conjunction with expanded livestock production.
2n.       Sorghum is the main feedgrain whose production is expected to
increase significantly under the Project. Sorghum production has doubled
since 1966 but market prices are still above the guaranteed price, as in-
creasing quantities are demanded for milk, pig, and poultry industry expansion.
Fruits and Vegetables
21.       The importance of Mexico as a supplier of fresh vegetables and
fruits to the U.S. markets has increased in recent years.  This trend should
continue for a number of commodities, although Mexico's projected future
capacity to supply export demands is not assured.



APPENDIX 1-2
Page 5
22.       Tomatoes continue to be the most important vegetable, with ex-
ports valued at around US$100 million per year, and Mexico is now supply-
ing over half of the fresh winter tomatoes (December to May season) being
marketed in the U.S. Costs of production and marketing have been rising in
both countries but more so in the U.S. (Florida), thereby strengthening
Mexico's advantage. Mexico should continue to increase exports of vine-
ripe tomatoes, while Florida production will probably decline. The total
U.S. market for fruits and vegetables is now close to US$200 million per
year.
23.       It is expected that Mexico will also find an increasing U.S.
market, especially for winter cucumbers, fresh peppers, and eggplant. More
recently, a few important foreign companies began to produce asparagus in
the Bahio region for the U.S. market, partly at the expense of strawberry
production.
24.       At present, the principal fruit exports are melons (mostly canta-
loupe) and strawberries, although potential markets exist for a rather
wide variety of other fruits (citrus, bananas, avocado, mango, tamarind,
guava, and such). Since U.S. production of cantaloupe is practically non-
existent during the cold months, Mexico enjoys a captive market in the
winter and early spring, despite rising costs of production and marketing.
Mexico also holds a comparative advantage over the U.S. in strawberries, so
that this export market should continue to expand.
March 22, 1973

APPENDIX 1-3

MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
AGRICULTURAL EDUCATION, RESEARCH, AND EXTENSION
A. Introduction
1.        Mexico's agricultural education, research, and extension services
have been expanding steadily for the past five years and are now regarded
as among the best in Latin America. In general, however, they are still
insufficient to cope with the needs of anything but a small proportion of
Mexico's farmers. Nevertheless, they provide a good basis on which to plan
future expansion.
B. Agricultural Education Services
Higher Education
2.        There are 15 agricultural undergraduate schools in Mexico.  Of
these, five are major schools with a combined enrollment of about 6,600
students which this year will award about 1,150 Ingenieros Agronomos degrees.
Standards vary between schools, and, in many cases, laboratory and library
facilities are inadequate, but, in general, it can be said that the main
weakness of most schools is their failure to provide practical orientation.
Three of these schools have postgraduate courses, namely, the National
School of Agriculture at Chapingo, the Technical Institute of Monterrey,
and the "Antonio Narro" Superior School of Agriculture. Chapingo, the
biggest and oldest postgraduate school, has nine departments offering
Masters Degrees and two have recently begun offering Doctors Degrees. The
Chapingo Graduate School has 230 students and, to date, 207 Masters Degrees
have been granted. It is estimated that about 8,000 students have obtained
primary degrees from these schools and about 25%, or 1,550, are employed
by either public or private farm credit institutions.
3.        There are nine veterinary schools in Mexico, with a total enroll-
ment of about 4,200 undergraduate students, about 700 of whom are expected
to graduate this year. The only postgraduate school is located at the
Mexican National University. In total, there are about 3,000 qualified
veterinary scientists registered in Mexico, and the fact that most of them
work in the clinical and animal diseases fields and very few directly in the
field of animal production reflects the relative emphasis placed on these
topics in all undergraduate courses.
4.        Most of the agricultural and veterinary colleges are financed
from federal and state sources but, in general, the funds are insufficient
to allow for anything but modest expansion and in some cases are even in-
adequate for current needs. Considerable financial help is necessary to



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 2
equip these colleges (and perhaps establsih others) so that they will be
able to produce the numbers of qualified personnel required to assist in the
urgently needed expansion of the country's agricultural output. In most
cases, however, additional finances are not the only constraint to the
effectiveness of higher agriculture/veterinary education in Mexico. Courses
are often inadequate and unrelated to the needs of the country, and, as was
stated earlier, too little emphasis is placed on practical studies which
are directly related to production problems.
5.        It is apparent that a study of the higher agricultural/veterinary
education in Mexico is needed and the terms of reference for any such work
should emphasize (a) reorganization of the system to best meet the rapidly
growing demands for agricultural products; (b) closer cooperation between
the various colleges, particularly those teaching agriculture and those
teaching veterinary science; and (c) closer cooperation between the various
federal and state organizations responsible for agricultural research, ex-
tension, credit, and land reform.
6.        The National Agricultural College is the best known and the biggest
of all the higher agricultural institutions in Mexico. Falling under the
administrative arm of SAG, it is unique, and, in addition to carrying out
its teaching programs, it also serves as a regional center for agricultural
research, training, demonstration, and extension. In this it has received
foreign support from the Rockefeller and Ford Foundations, USIAD, IDB, and
FAO/UNDP.  The FONDO has drawn heavily on Chapingo for recruiting its staff
and the college offers a special course for technicians from the FONDO and
public and private institutions associated with the FONDO program.  The
FONDO has also recently established a "residencia" at Chapingo to coordinate
activities of the various institutions engaged in agricultural and livestock
research and to promote the rapid application of relevant research findings
at the farm level.
Sub-professional Agricultural Education
7.        There are seven technical agricultural and livestock schools in
Mexico financed jointly by SAG and state governments. The student popula-
tion is about 2,400 and about 700 graduate yearly. These schools have
difficulty in finding and keeping competent staff, and employment opportun-
ities of the graduates within public and private institutions have been
limited because of a lack of appreciation of the role of sub-professionals
as technical assistants to augment the effectiveness of the better qualified
professional graduate employees. This situation, however, is now changing
within the FONDO, which proposes to employ a number of sub-professional
agricultural technicians to work under the direction of its experienced
professional credit agronomists. If this trial is successful in releasing
the professional for more on-farm advisory work, the FONDO could become an
important employer of the sub-professional agriculturalist.
8.        In addition to technical training, vocational courses in farm and
livestock husbandry are available at 159 secondary schools where three-year
courses are given in farming, livestock husbandry, or the handling and



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 3
maintenance of farm machinery and equipment.  Unfortunately, qualified
teachers are few and these schools suffer from lack of facilities, partic-
ularly those needed to teach livestock husbandry.
C. Research and Demonstration
9.        The two major agricultural research institutions in Mexico are the
Instituto Nacional de Investigaciones Agricolas (INIA) and the Instituto
Nacional de Investigaciones Pecuarias (INIP). Both are located in SAG,
administered respectively by the Under Secretaries of Agriculture and Live-
stock, but there is little coordination between them. Some research
specifically related to the activities of a number of other institutions
such as the National Forestry Service, Institute for Improvement of Sugar
Production, Mexican Coffee Institute, Ministry of Hydraulic Resources (SRIH),
the Center for Agrarian Research, and the Mexican Institute of Technological
Research is also carried out, and programs are underway at a number of
universities, mainly at those with graduate schools. The Rockefeller Founda-
tion has given significant support to agricultural research in Mexico since
1943, and has provided a demonstration and training function over the past
25 years which has influenced greatly the establishment and expansion of
most of the research institutions at present in existence, particularly
IIA and INIP.  Government plans to continue to support both these institu-
tions and proposes a growth rate of about 20% for at least the next few
years.
Crop Research
10.       Most of the research to date in this field has been concentrated
in the irrigated areas, with little being done in the more extensive dry
land regions. INIA has started to correct this imbalance, however, and now
has eight regional research centers and 35 experimental stations, compared
with six regional centers and 21 experimental stations in 1969. All are
located in the main ecological areas of the country. The centers and ex-
perimental stations carry out research in genetics, plant pathology, entom-
ology, soils and fertilizers, and general agronomy in relation to a wide
range of cereal, horticultural, oil seeds, forage, and pasture crops. Of
the present research staff of 456 graduates, 32 are Ph.D.'s, 55 M.Sc.'s,
and the remainder are Ingenieros Agronomos. INIA's budget, which increased
from Mex$43 million in 1969 to Mex$75 million in 1972, is expected to rise
a further 35% in 1973.
11.       The Rockefeller Foundation made a particularly outstanding con-
tribution to Mexican agricultural research in the breeding and development
of basic food cereal crops. Its high yielding Mexican-bred wheats, for
example, are rapidly replacing traditional lower yielding wheats in many
countries. In maize breeding also, the high lysine varieties bred in
Mexico hold the promise of improved nutritional standards in the lower
income sectors of many countries where maize is the staple food. In spite
of this excellent work, however, it is alarming to note that in 1971 only



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 4
30% of the wTheat and 9% of the maize areas in Mexico were planted with im-
proved seeds.  Of concern also is the fact that, in 1972, Mexico once again
became a net importer of wheat and maize after a number of years as a net
exporter. A major reason for this is that farmers in the dry farming areas
have been much less ready to accept improved seeds than have farmers with
irrigation, which indicates that more studies are needed on seed adaptability
to non-irrigated areas before a change can be expected.
12.       While it is recognized that Mexican crop research has achieved much
over the past decade, it has been deficient in a number of important areas.
Relatively little work, for example, has been done on the adaptation and
-Ltegration of individual research findings into farming systems within the
context of the needs of Mexican agriculture. This work not only involves
the quantification of the effects of new inputs (such as seeds and fertil-
izers) on outputs in physical and financial terms, but their integration
into the crop (and perhaps pasture) rotation patterns which will ensure and
sustain a high level of production while maximizing returns. Such programs
are needed in both rainfed and irrigation areas. Also, in the irrigation
areas, much more work needs to be done on on-farm water reticulation systems,
layout, and designs. Quantitative data is also needed on the efficiency of
water use expressed in terms of yields per unit of water applied, since the
supply of water is a major limiting factor in almost all of the irrigation
districts in the country.
13.       With the growing support being given to IMLA by Government, it is
apparent that this organization is going to assume an increasingly important
role in performing the country's agricultural research.  It would be unwise,
however, to try to push expansion beyond the capacity to recruit and train
a high caliber staff since the quality of work depends more on the quality
of staff than on its size. In this context, it is therefore essential that
the proportion of Ph.D. and M.Sc. graduates be increased. However, because
it is doubtful that existing graduate schools will be able to fulfill all
these needs immediately, adequate funds for overseas scholarships will be
needed for some time.
14.       INIA should also put much more effort into production-oriented
research, both in crops and animals, planning its programs so that they are
closely coordinated with the research activities of other institutions,
particularly INIP, SRH, and FONDO. Finally, INIA should maintain a close
relationship with the country's extension services to ensure (a) the rapid
application of research findings at the farm level, and (b) an effective
clhannel for rapid feedback of production problems on which to base research
programs more closely related to the needs of the farmer.
Livestock Research
1,.       INIP is the major organization responsible for Mexican livestock
research. It currently has a professional staff of 130 (including five
Ph.D.'s and 15 Masters) whose expertise includes genetics, nutrition, re-
production, forage and pasture management, diseases and parasites, extension,
economics, seriology, and the routine production of vaccines. In addition



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 5
to the main Palo Alto laboratories in Ilexico City, field facilities are
provided at 10 experimental stations located in most of the major animal
production areas in the country. The institute budget expanded significantly.
from Mex$5 million in 1965 to Mex$50 million in 1972, while the proposed
expenditure for 1973 is Mex$60 million. Even so, the resources of INIP fall
far short of meeting the research needs of the livestock suhsector. In
addition to financial constraints, INTP's programs in the past have been
oriented mainly to the fields of preventive medicine vaccine production and
genetics, and, with the exception of poultry, little has yet been done in
the study of animal production/management problems more closely related to
irmediate farm or ranch needs.
16.       The lack of data from soundly designed and executed production-
oriented research is far more serious with livestock than with crops.  Of
highest priority is research in the wet and dry tropical regions, where,
based on experience from similar environments in other tropical countries
(notably Australia), production of beef and milk could be increased many
times through better use of pasture, involving both the introduction of new
species and more efficient management of existing pastures. Although a
start has been made by FONDO, INIA, and INIP, with simple trials to test new
legumes and grasses and observe major fertilizer responses, there is an
urgent need for more critical studies to quantify animal production responses
from improved and natural pastures. This involves studying the interactions
between soil, plant, and animal under a wide range of conditions and develop-
ing new pasture management techniques which will sustain production at the
highest and most economic level.
17.       A few well designed and managed stocking rate experiments to
measure the production potential of sown and natural pastures under various
fertilizer regimes would provide most of the data needed to establish a
basis for making more specific recommendations on new production/management
svstems to farmers and ranchers. Many facilities are already available to
execute this work, particularly if the resources of INIA, INIP, and FONDO
were coordinated. INIA and INIP have adequate field stations located within
the wet and dry tropics at which these experiments could be carried out and
both institutions and FONDO have sufficient pasture agronomists, who, with
a minimum of help in initial experimental designs and establishment, would
be fully capable of executing these studies. Thus, under the proposed
Project, funds have been allocated to establish about 1,000 ha of grazing
trials and to set up a supporting plant nutrition unit.  Provision has also
been made to retain the services of a tropical pasture/animal production
expert and a plant nutrition specialist as consultants.
18.       It is proposed that the work be carried out as a collaborative
effort between INIA, INIP, and FONDO and that the funds, which should be
provided bv Government as an interest-free grant, be administered by FONDO,
with the research studies being a part of the normal programs of INIA and
INIP. The workc would be organized through the new FONDO technical coordi-
nation "residencia" at Chapingo (para 6). The conduct of these studies would



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 6
bring additional benefits in that the resources of the three institutions
would be concentrated in a large collaborative program for the first time,
and there would be valuable opportunities for training research and exten-
sion technicians in the performance of production studies.
Role of FONDO in Livestock Research and Demonstration
19.       Although its activities in these fields are minor relative to its
main function as a crediit cum technical service institution, FONDO has carried
out a number of important programs with its own staff and resources to provide
technical information needed for better servicing of farmers and ranchers
participating in the Project. The most outstanding has been a research pro-
gram on low cost calf rearing in the arid, semi-arid, and temperate dairy
regions with the objective of encouraging farmers to rear their own calves
and thereby reduce the need to continue the currently large and costly im-
portation of milk cows. The cost of this program, designed and executed by
a young FONTDO nutrition specialist, has been less than US$200,000, which is
small compared with the potential benefits. If the results of the work were
applied countrywide, involving a number of similar calf-rearing ce-nters,
the annual savings to the dairy industry would be at least US$25 million.
This is a prime example of the value of well designed and executed production-
oriented studies.
20.       In addition to its tropical pasture program in the wet and dry
tropics, FONDO has also supported the establishment of The Mexican Society
of Animal Production's Training Center for Animal Production by financing
k.ey staff and some facilities.  Although this support has been of great
assistance, it nonetheless has fallen far short of that provided for in
the Third Project, in which it was envisaged that FONDO would assume the
major responsibility for equipping and developing the Center through free
grants. Unless more help is forthcoming, therefore, it is unlikely that
the Center will be able to provide effectively the much needed training
facilities in tropical pasture/animal management for FONDO technicians as
planned.
21.       FONDO is also in the process of establishing two farms to demon-
strate new techniques for dairy and crop production in wet and dry tropical
regions. One of these is located on an ejido in Tabasco and the other in
the LIPSP project area at Altamirano. It is proposed that the latter, being
set tip in collaboration with SRH, serve as a center for the study of low
cost farm building methods, pastures, and tropical dairy cross-breeding
programs as yet untried in Mexico, as well as for the training of technicians
and ejidatarios.
D. Agricultural Extension
22.       Extension services in Mexico are provided by the Federal and State
Agricultural Extension Services, SRH, the FONDO, and other associated public
and private credit institutions. In the specialized irrigation areas pro-
ducing fruits and vegetables, the larger farmers also often employ their



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 7
own professionals to provide technical services for their operations. Ex-
tension services, however, are still inadequate to meet the needs of Mexico's
2.82 million farmers. The Federal Service, the biggest of these, now has
about 1,840 officers, 1,500 of which are graduates in either agriculture
or veterinary science and 340 are sub-professionals, but these numbers are
sufficient to service effectively only a very small percentage of the
agricultural sector. SRH extension services are concentrated specifically
in irrigation areas, while the FONDO, with 397 graduate credit technicians,
and the associated public and private credit institutes, with about the
same number, provide planning and supervisory sercices to farmers receiving
credits under the FONDO programs.
23.       Government has realized the need for improving its extension
services and last year increased the budget for the Federal Service by 88%.
The service was also reorganized within SAG, raising it to the Directorate
General level, and a new highly qualified director was appointed. Addition-
ally, extension workers' salaries have been made more competitive within
the agricultural profession, which should reduce the high turnover of staff,
a serious obstacle to building an efficient organization.
24.       To date extension services of both SAG and SRII have been concen-
trated in the irrigation areas, while the dry land and cropping and ranch-
ing areas have been relatively neglected. The FONDO and its associated
public and private credit institution technicians have also tended to be
concentrated in the centers of highest agricultural production although
services are provided in all areas of the country where FONIDO farm credit is
disbursed.
25.       In general, the existing extension services have not been suf-
ficiently effective in introducing new production techniques and management
methods, even among the farmers with whom technicians have worked.  An
important reason for this has been the large proportion of inexperienced
young staff in the ranks of the SAG and SRH services resulting from the
high turnover rate of professional staff.  Lack of transport for field
officers has also been a serious drawback, particularly in the SAG service,
but this is now being corrected by the introduction of a scheme whereby SAG
officers will be helped to purchase vehicles and paid mileage when on duty.
26.       Although the problems mentioned have not been serious within the
FONDO service, which is recognized as having the best team of credit/exten-
sion specialists in the country, its performance to date in extending new
production and management technology to its borrowers has been disappointing.
It was shown in a recent survey that the investment of more than US$250
million, under the First and Second Livestock and Agriculture Projects,
had a marked impact on increasing production and profits, but these gains
were "primarily as a result of scale of expansion rather than improved
production efficiency" (Annex 5). There is little doubt that they would
have been even greater had the FONDO technicians devoted more time to
technical supervision. Although most FONDO technicians recognize this
shortcoming and would like to do more technical/advisory work with borrowers,



APPENDIX 1-3
Page 8
they are restricted because of the burden of administrative and routine
responsibilities.  Many also complain of the lack of sound quantitative in-
formation, applicable to their districts, on which to base their recommenda-
tions. The FONDO administration is slowly realizing the significance of
these findings and has taken some steps to correct the situation. The
recruiting of sub-professionals to help the graduate technicians (para 7)
is one of these. Also, the FONDO has become directly involved in a number
of research and demonstration programs, mainly low cost calf rearing and
tropical pasture development, directly related to the needs of its program.
27.       To summarize, it is apparent that there is an urgent need for
goaatly expanded and better coordinated extension services in Mexico. Recent
moves by Government to upgrade SAG and provide better facilities are promising
as are also the moves being made to achieve better coordination between FONDO,
SAG, and SRP. However, these improvements, which are yet to be put into ef-
fect, will have little lasting impact unless SAG and SRH are prepared to (a)
offer career opportunities of trained agricultural professionals equal at all
times to alternative fields; (b) improve in-service training opportunities and
facilities; and (c) maintain closer contract with agricultural and veterinary
education and research services and influence them to becoming more closely
oriented to the priority needs of Mexican agriculture.
March 22, 1973

MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FONDO DE GARANTIA Y FOMENTO PARA LA
AGRICULTURA, GANADERIA Y AVICULTURA
A. Objectives and Powers
1.        The Fondo de Garantia y Fomento para la Agricultura, Ganaderia y
Avicultura (FONDO) was established in 1955 by the Government of Mexico
(Government) to provide a combination of funds and technical assistance
necessary to make agricultural and livestock development effective in Mexico.
FONDO is empowered to:
(a) extend loans to private credit institutions and regional
Banco Agropecarios (regional BANAGROs) so that they may,
in turn, grant credit to livestock and agricultural
producers;
3                  (b)  rediscount paper of private credit institutions and
regional BANAGROs, representing loans of all maturities
to such producers;
(c) guarantee the repayment of loans that private credit
institutions and regional BANAGROs may grant to such
producers; and
(d) carry out such other operations as may be necessary in
the furtherance of livestock and agricultural development.
2.        Because it was determined that lending operations should be con-
ducted on a business-like basis, private banks were designated, at the outset,
as channels for FONDO. In 1965, the first regional BANAGROs were authorized
to use FONDO rediscounting facilities.
3.        In August 1972, concomitant with its stepped-up program for develop-
ment of ejidatarios and other low-income producers, Government created the
Special Fund for Technical Assistance and Guarantee for Agricultural Credits
(FEGA). The purpose of FEGA is:
(a) to organize, provide, and defray the cost of identification
evaluation, technical assistance, and supervision services
required for agricultural production projects designed to
improve income of ejidatarios and small-holders, and;



ANNEX 2
Page 2
(b)  to guarantee the recovery of up to 60%, and in special                         0
cases, 80%, of principal balances due and unpaid of farm
loans granted by private credit institutions for projects
that are appraised as technically feasible and economically
viable and supervised as provided for under (a) above.
The Government has started FEGA with a contribution of Mex$25 million (US$2.0
million).
B. Organization and Management
4.        In general, policies and regulations are set for FONDO by a Techni-
cal Committee composed of ten members: one representative each from the
iMinistry of Finance and Public Credit; the Ministry of Agriculture and Live-
stock; the Banco de Mexico (BANXICO); the National Bank for Foreign Commerce;
the Banco Nacional Agropecuario (the holding company for regional BANAGROs
headquartered in Mexico City); the National Insurance Company for Agriculture
and Livestock; the Bankers' Association; and a representative each of farmers,
ranchers, and ejidatarios. However, those policies and regulations concerning
the operations of the IBRD-assisted projects and those financed with IDB help
(Special Fund) are determined by a smaller Technical Committee composed of
six members: two from the Ministry of Finance and Public Credit, two from
the Banco de Mexico, one from the Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock, and
one from the Banco Nacional Agropecuario.
5.        On the other hand, policies and regulations for FEGA are set
by a Technical. Committee of ten members: two from the Ministry of Finance
and Public Credit, and one each from the lfinistry of Agriculture and Livestock,
BANXtICO, Banco Nacional Agropecuario, Banco Ejidal, Banco Agricola, the
National Insurance Company for Agriculture and Livestock, the National Fund
for Ejidal Development, and the National Farmers Federation.
6.        Fnhe powers of the technical committees for the lending activities
of the FONDO (paras 1 and 4) are:
(a)  to authorize the kind and scope of operations, in general;
(b)  to establisn interest rates, premia, and other charges to
be collected on all transactions;
(c)  to approve the annual expenditure budget; and
(d) to exercise such other powers as may be entrusted to them
by lawful decrees, contracts, or trust agreement.
0



ANNEX 2
Page 3
7.        The powers of the Technical Committee for FEGA involve the
following:
(a) formulation of rules to govern FEGA's operations;
(b) examination and approval of the annual expenditure budget,
the programs and cost of technical services, and the
expenses incurred in implementing FEGA's guarantee
operations;
(c) specifying the general conditions under which agreements
to be signed by the trustee (in this case, BANXICO) for
the fulfullment of the purposes of the trust; and
(d) such other powers as may be entrusted to it by Government.
8.        The administration of FONDO's activities (including that of the
Special Fund and FEGA) is under the general supervision of BANXICO, as
trustee.  The FONDO has its own office facilities and personnel, and opera-
tions are carried out under the immediate responsibility of a full-time
director. He is assisted by two sub-directors: one in charge of general
services (administration, accounting, and statistics) and another in charge
of technical services and assistance. Two technical coordinators serve
under the latter sub-director: one in charge of three groups of experts in
the head office (agriculture, livestock, and agro-industries), and another
in charge of the FONDO's network of field offices. A Division of Planning
and Operations functions immediately under the director and is responsible
for studies. All personnel appointments are made by BANXICO, and FONDO
employees enjoy the same benefits as those allowed BANXICO employees. FONDO's
organization chart appears in WB5553(2R), while an organogram, showing the
functions of the various FONDO units, is given in WB5552(2R).
9.        FONDO's head office is in Mexico City.  In the field, it maintains
an adequate network of 70 offices and agencies, supervised by six regional
offices.
C. Technical Services
10.       Because of its commitment to technical evaluation of and assistance
to farm projects, FONDO has vigorously pursued expansion of its technical
staff. From a total of about 135 technicians of varying agricultural dis-
ciplines in 1965, the staff rose steadily: to about 200 by December 1968,
to 320 in November 1970, and to 465 in November 1972. Three hundred and
ninety two, or 84% of the total, are assigned to field offices. FONDO
recruits about 50 to 55 top university graduates per year, who then undergo
training, with emphasis on agricultural credit, before assuming their duties.
Likewise, seminars and short courses are given to permanent staff and overseas
training provided to selected staff.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
11.       Field technicians evaluate loans, verify borrower's use of loan
proceeds, and render technical advice and assistance. Loan evaluation in-
volves an examination of the borrower's operation before the contemplated
investments and an appraisal of the technology and management to be adopted
once the loan is made and also of the proposed investments to be made. At
present, 75% to 80%, on the average, of a field technician's time is involved
in loan evaluation.
D. Lending Operations and Source of Funds
12.       FONDO makes short-, medium-, and long-term loans in three areas of
operations:
(a) the ALPRO (Alliance for Progress) Program, financed from
USAID and IDB loans, which provides medium- and long-term
funds to low-income farmers and ejidatarios at relatively
low rates of interest;
(b) IBRD-assisted projects (Loans 430-ME, 610-ME, and 747-ME),
which provide medium- and long-term finance for all types
of farm and agro-industry investments at market rates of
interest; and
(c)  short-term rediscounting program involving complementary
production finance to the first two programs.
ALPRO Program
13.       Under the ALPRO Program, loans are made only to borrowers that
engage in farming as a major activity; have farming assets of no more than
three times the amount of the loan (loans are limited to no more than
Mex$150,000, or in exceptional cases, up to Mex$250,000), and earn an annual
gross income of no more than Mex$100,000 (US$8,000). Loans are based on
technically and financially evaluated plans and are of 2 to 15 years maturity.
Primary borrowers pay 7.6% for loans, while participating credit institutions,
in turn, pay 4% to 4.6% on rediscount funds, depending on the portion of the
amount refinanced by FONDO. Rediscounts are limited to 70% to 90% of a loan
granted.
14.       The ALPRO Program has been assisted by two loans from USAID totalling
US$41.5 million:  the first one in 1963 for US$20 million, at 3/4 of 1% per
annum payable in 40 years and the other for US$21.5 million, in 1966, at 3.5%
per annum for 25 years. To continue the Program, IDB, in 1968, extended a
loan of US$20 million at 3% per annum for 25 years and another in 1971 for
US$32 million, also for 25 years at 3% per annum.
*S



ANNEX 2
Page 5
15.       From the inception of the Program to September 30, 1972, a total
of about Mex$848.4 million (US$67.9 million) in rediscounts had been granted
to refinance primary loans of approximately Mex$942.7 million (US$75.4
million). Loan collections are being reloaned and outstanding loans under
the Program have remained at a more or less constant level of about Mex$450
to 500 million (US$36 to 40 million) during the last three years.
IBRD-assisted Projects
16.       Lending operations under projects assisted by Loans 430-ME, 610-ME,
and 747-HE are discussed in detail in Annex 5.
Short-Term Rediscounting Progran
17.       Projects assisted by FONDO must demonstrate ability to secure work-
ing capital. To this end, participating banks are encouraged by FONDO to
provide the short-term financing necessary and, accordingly, they operate a
rediscounting program designed to refinance such assistance. Rediscount
funds are at 6 to 8% per annum (depending on portion being rediscounted),
while loans to farmers are at no more than 12% per annum.
18.       Funds for short-term operations come mainly from rediscounts from
BANXICO and from collections of loans. The FONDO short-term rediscount
portfolio has been consistently increasing and reached about Mex$524 million
(US$41.9 million) on September 30, 1972, as compared to Mex$120 million on
December 31, 1966. This fact may be considered as evidence of production
growth in participating farms.
E. Financial and Operating Conditions
19.       Total income has grown as FONDO operations have expanded.  Whereas
in 1966 total income was only Mex$51.3 million, in 1969 it more than doubled
to Mex$106.3 million and almost quadrupled to Mex$185.1 million in 1971.
During the first three quarters of 1972, total income was Mex$167.2 million,
which should be about Mex$220 million for the entire year of 1972. Interest
from rediscounts is the main source of income and has constituted during the
last three years around 75 to 90% of the total.
20.       FONDO's expenses have, likewise, increased as its operations have
grown, with interest on money borrowed showing the most significant gain.
Whereas, in 1966, this item was only about 21% of total income during the last
three years, it has ranged from 52 to 59% of total income, due mainly to the
higher costs of funds from IBRD.  However, it is noteworthy that salaries
and employee benefits, inspite of maintained expansion of technical staff,
have remained at around 28 to 30% of total income.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
21.       FONDO operates as a low-profit organization in achieving the objec-
tives for which it was created. Total income has been sufficient to cover
expenses. Details of FONDO operations from January 1, 1968 to September 30,
1972, are shown in Appendix 2-1.
22.       FONDO is in good financial condition (Appendix 2-2).  Trust principal
(Government equity, including accumulated net income) has grown from Mex$120
million (US$9.6 million) on December 31, 1966, to Mex$264.7 million (US$21.2
million) on December 31, 1972.  The increase is due largely to contributions
by Government to the Special Fund (para 4) of about Mex$99.8 million during
1q66-72 after the original contribution of M1ex$5 million in 1965 (para 24).
23.       FONDO has not provided for possible losses from bad debts, but such
a provision is not deemed necessary because FONDO's risk assets represent
claims against sound banking institutions whose financial condition is periodi-
cally monitored by BANXICO. Moreover, the operations of these credit insti-
tutions (actually all Mexican banking institutions) are examined, under the
law, by the National Banking Commission. All FONDO rediscounts have been
paid at maturity.
24.       During fiscal years 1973 to 1977, which covers the proposed dis-
bursement period of the Fourth Project, FONDO's financial operating results
are expected to be maintained at least at the same level as in the past
(Appendix 2-3) and its financial condition to remain good (Appendix 2-4).
Government contributions will increase during this period and beyond because
of Government commitment to repay IBRD loans, with such repayments (which
FONDO would otherwise have made) to be considered as additional Government
contributions to the Special Fund.
25.       FONDO's accounting system is satisfactory.  Auditing is performed
by an independent unit responsible to the Auditing Department of BANXICO.
In addition the accounts are audited by an independent private auditing firm.
May 25, 1973
