BANCO NACIONAL DE CREDITO EJIDAL, S.A.
A. Introduction
1.        The Banco National de Credito Ejidal (Banco Ejidal) was established
in 1935 by the Government of Mexico to provide funds as well as technical and
other assistance to ejidatarios. Its main functions are to:
(a) establish local credit societies, or ejidos, and finance,
through these societies, the agricultural and livestock
activities of ejidatarios;
(b) provide technical and economic guidance to ejidatarios,
while ensuring that their activities are in line with the
general aims of Government agricultural policy;
(c) facilitate the supply of farm inputs at minimum cost and
the disposal of farm produce at maximum prices; and
(d) establish agro-industries as may be desirable to maximize
the value of farm products and the returns to ejidatarios.
B.  Organization and Ianagement
2.        General policies and regulations are set by a Board of Directors,
headed by the Secretary of the Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock and
composed of representatives of the Ministry of Agriculture and Livestock,
the Ministry of Agrarian Affairs and Colonization, the Ministry of Finance
and Public Credit, the Banco de Mexico, the National Bank for Foreign Com-
merce, and the Banco Nacional de Credito Agricola.
3.        The operations of Banco Ejidal are carried out under the direction
of a General Manager and an Assistant General Manager, who are assisted in
their administrative and planning duties by three recently established ad-
visory units: the Administrative Commission, the Organization Methods Unit
and the Programming Unit. Except for the Controller's Department, which
reports directly to the General Manager, all head office departments, branches,
agencies, and zone offices, as well as the three Bancos Agrarios (wholly-
owned subsidiaries of Banco Ejidal), report through the Assistant General
Manager.
4.        The Banco's assistance to ejidatarios is monitored largely by its
five operating departments: Credit, Organization, Commercial, Rural
Engineering, and Industrial Plants; while the service departments, i.e.,
Administration, Accounting, Data Processing, Economic Studies, Legal, and
Treasurer, are primarily engaged in performing staff functions.
0

C. Lending Operations and Sources of Funds
6.        The Banco Ejidal makes three basic types of loans, which may be
defined as follows:
(a) loans to Ejidos, or local credit societies:
(i)  Short-term agricultural production and livestock loans,
for periods not in excess of one year, to assist their
members in financing production expenses in seeds, fer-
tilizers, insecticides, irrigation water, small repairs
and the like;
(ii) Medium- and long-term agricultural and livestock loans
for land clearing, pasture improvement, small irrigation
works, plantations, silos, dwellings, machinery and
equipment, breeding stock and the like.
(b)  Working capital and investment loans to ejidos for the estab-
lishment, expansion, and operation of warehousing and indus-
trial processing facilities and for the establishment of
cattle fattening yards and the like.
Rate of Interest
7.        Until late 1972, the rate of interest applicable to all types of
loans was 10% per annum. In a few special cases, a higher or lower rate
might be charged, but only with the specific approval of the Board of Directors.
Now, however, the interest rate is 7.6% to conform with the legal rate to
low-income producers established by the Bank of Mexico.

8.        The volume of business carried out by the members of the Banco
Ejidal system during the past four years was as follows:

TOTAL                         1         12440       7      2,141
Overdue Loans
9.        While the available information on overdue accounts is fragmentary,
recent data for the Banco Ejidal proper, exclusive of the Bancos Agrarios,
points to an improvement in the collection of loans over the past four years:
Loan portfolio at end of year for Banco Ejidal proper
In good standing      Overdue    % Overdue
(Millions of Mex$)
1968                     1,121             2,403          68.2
1969                     1,121             2,532         59.6
1970                     1,963             2,740          58.3
1971                     2,769             2,930          51.4
10.       Over the years, the Government has directed Banco Ejidal to make
credit funds available for projects, inspired by social or political consider-
ations, although it was realized at the outset by all concerned that such
advances would, at best, be only partially recovered. The present Government
has recognized that such projects should be financed by outright Government
grants and, furthermore, that farm development costs for such things as land
clearing and leveling, road construction, fencing, well digging and small
irrigation works ought, in the case of ejidatarios, to be financed from
Government subsidies. In line with this, an important land clearing and
development program (paras 20 and 21) has just been announced. These changes
in Government policy should be reflected in an improved loan collection rate
of Banco Ejidal, especially since the latter, under new management (para 22)
is also directing considerably more attention to the collection of due and
overdue loans. Future financial statements should, therefore, show a rapidly
reducing overdue loans balance.
Sources of Funds
11.       The volume of lending of Banco Ejidal is determined by the Bank
each year, in consultation with several Government ministries, on the basis
of analysis of farm plans and the availability of lending resources, and



ANNEX 3
Page 4
the figure must be approved by Government before it becomes final. Approxi-
mately 75% of the funds required by Banco Ejidal for short-term production
loans is borrowed (at 8% interest) from the Banco Nacional de Comercio
Exterior; 20% is obtained as a grant from the Government of Mexico; and 5%
represents the portion which must be re-deposited by borrowers. In the case
of funds required for the medium- and long-term lending program, main sources
are Government grants and borrowings from the private sector; these sources
are complemented by the Banco Ejidal's own resources.
D. Technical and Other Services to Ejidatarios
Technical Services
12.       The Banco Ejidal provides some technical assistance to ejidatarios
through its 1,774 field inspectors, only 133 of whom are qualified agrono-
mists. These inspectors are primarily engaged in loan-use supervision (ad-
vising the Banco when the various tranches of approved credits should be
disbursed and verifying the borrowers' use of loan proceeds) and they have
little time to devote to technical assistance activities. Other assistance
is provided by the Banco's Rural Engineering Department which helps Ejida-
tarios in solving technical problems associated with the mechanization of
their farm operations and with irrigation. Additionally, it guides ejida-
tarios in defining their requirements and provides training to some members
in the operation and maintenance of machinery and equipment, thus ensuring
satisfactory returns on investment. Finally, the newly formed Organization
Department is responsible for organizing and reorganizing local credit
societies.
Commercial Services
13.       The bank's Commercial Department assists borrowers in purchasing
inputs and equipment at minimum prices and in selling their produce at
maximum prices by eliminating dealers and other intermediaries. Data on
the volume of commercial business carried out by the Bank for ejidatarios
is not available, but losses on this activity have averaged Mex$3.5 million
per annum over the past four years.
Agro-industries
14.       In October 1972, 156 agro-industrial plants, either owned or
financed by Banco Ejidal, were engaged in processing the surplus production
of ejidatarios and nine additional plants were under construction. The most
important categories were as follows:
41 coffee plants               8 chile dehydrating plants
39 sisal plants                6 sugar mills
20 cotton seed plants          5 resin extraction plants
9 rice mills



ANNEX 3
Page 5
0           Some 70 of these units are now owned by associations of ejidatarios, which
have purchased them with the financial assistance of Banco Ejidal. It is
expected that, in time, the others will also be acquired by such associations.
15.       The volume of business of the bank-owned industrial units increased
from Mex$143 million in 1968 to Mex$174 million in 1971, and consistent net
losses over the period have averaged about 41% of gross income. A serious
effort is currently being made to reduce operating expenses and some indus-
trial units may be closed down as a result of the analysis of the operational
data which is presently being carried out.
E. Operating Results and Financial Condition
16.       Total banking income, other than that received as Government
subsidies, has fallen rather sharply since 1969. Interest earned and col-
lected is, of course, an important component and it is noteworthy that,
while the effective rate of interest on the bank's loans was 10% per annum,
interest collected, expressed as a percentage of the bank's total loans
outstanding (including overdue loans), fluctuated between only 2.4% and 3.5%
per annum during tihe 1968-72 period. In fact, the bank has not even collected
an interest of 10% on its "loans in good standing" during this period.  On
the other hand, interest paid by Banco Ejidal, expressed as a percentage of
the bank's commitments, fluctuated between 5.6% and 7.3%.  It can, there-
fore, be seen that unless considerably more vigor is exercised in the col-
lection of interest, uncollected earnings will continue to be a source of
considerable losses to the bank.
17.       Total banking expenses increased quite sharply from 1969 to 1971
due mainly to a substantial rise in the provision for bad debts. General
expenses have risen by about 50% during this period, but have not increased
in relation to the volume of business transacted. The operating data shown
for 1972 is not comparable to that for the previous four years because it
does not include information on the three Bancos Agrarios and also because
there is no provision for bad debts and depreciation.
18.       Total losses on banking operations have thus amounted to Mex$2,058
million in the four-year period 1968-71 and if Government subsidies shown
as banking income are added, the figure becomes Mex$3,218 million. Adding
losses from commercial and industrial operations, the total amounts to
Mex$3,504 million (US$280 million).
19.       Despite this, Banco Ejidal's net worth has risen, owing to sub-
stantial Government injection of equity funds. In the three-year period
1969 to 1971, Government equity contributions, totaling Mex$2,918 million,
brought the net worth position from Mex$(26) million at the end of 1968 to
Mex$1,382 million at the end of 1971. The reserve for losses in past-due
loans and other miscellaneous receivables which, at the end of 1971, cor-
responded to 69% of the value of these assets, is believed to be adequate
tQ cover probable-osses,.,



ANNEX 3
Page 6
F.  Steps being Taken to Improve the Bank's Performance
20.       It is quite clear that the Banco Ejidal's main problem is its
unsatisfactory rate of loan collection. As mentioned earlier, one of the
principal reasons for the poor loan collection record is that the bank has
been called upon to finance, on a credit basis, agricultural programs which
the Government should have financed from subsidy funds. There is one hopeful
sign, however, that Government policies are becoming more realistic in this
regard. A National Land Clearing Program was recently announced that would
bring into agricultural production heretofore unproductive areas and entrust
them to groups of ejidatarios. Under this program, which will be administered
by Banco Ejidal, some 310,000 ha of timberland (including 124,000 ha in Oaxaca,
Jalisco and Nayarit) will be cleared in 1973 and a total of 1 million ha will
be cleared during the President's current term of office.
21.       The land clearing costs 1/ of the 1973 portion of this program will
be Mex$303 million, which will be paid for from Government budgetary re-
sources, as will the cost of complementary works (drains, access roads,
fences, grass planting, wells, sheds and the like) estimated at Mex$322 mil-
lion. The ejidos will thus receive it fully prepared and seeded and (in the
case of livestock producers) without having to incur any debt. Their bor-
rowing needs for machinery, cattle and such will then be met by Banco Ejidal
and will total an estimated Mex$433 million in medium-term credits, plus,
of course, short-term production loans as required.
22.       Some of the organizational, credit, data processing and administra-
tive reforms, which have taken shape during the past six months, while aimed
primarily at improving the general efficiency of the Banco, should help
considerably in improving its loan granting and collection and performance:
(a) A survey of the bank's present customers was carried out in
July and August 1972, to determine the financial position and
hence the borrowing capacity of ejidos and ejidatarios.
WThile this information will be used first to determine what
portion of the bank's overdue loans should be written off, it
will also help determine the maximum amount of any future
advances which could be made to these borrowers. Thus, it
should be an important instrument for improving the evaluation
of loan requests.
(b) Credit Planning and Study Commissions are being set up in all
branches and agencies of the bank in order to improve the
analysis of the viability of potential borrowers and to caution
those whose repayment record is unsatisfactory.
1/   Historically, loans for this purpose have been the most difficult to collect.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(c) The reduction in Banco Ejidal's administrative and accounting
personnel, which will be effected through the mechanization
of work systems and the computerization of data collection
and processing, will be offset by an increase in field per-
sonnel. The greater number of inspectors and the fact that
their duties will be redefined and they will be given more
facilities to carry them out should greatly increase the
amount of technical assistance to ejidatarios and, hence,
should improve their earnings and their ability to repay
their loan commitments.
(d) The bank's newly created Department of Organization will
reshape all existing credit societies in such a way that
the Banco will have more effective control of their crops
or livestock, which are the main sources of loan repayment
funds. Furthermore, the bank has decided to enlist presidents
of ejidos to assist field inspectors in the recuperation of
loan advances and to remunerate them for this service.
23.       Finally, the Government's decision, announced on October 24,
1972, to make funds (Mex$500 million a year initially) available to Banco
Ejidal through the FONDO for the first time in the bank's history, for
term loans to low-income ejidatarios at a low rate of interest (7.6%)
will give the Fondo a considerable say in shaping the term-lending activ-
ities, and indeed the entire operations of Banco Ejidal. The simultaneous
announcements that (a) all agricultural credit societies will be reorganized
in order to operate on a basis satisfactory to the FONDO, and (b) the over-
due loans portfolio of Banco Ejidal will be carefully analyzed are clear in-
dications that the Government wants to ensure, not only that credit facilities
for ejidatarios be expanded, but also that the quality and standard of that
assistance and of the institution which will provide it, be improved.
March 22, 1973

BANCO NACIONAL DE CREDITO AGRICOLA, S.A.
A.  Introduction
1.        The Banco Nacional de Credito Agricola (Banco Agricola), established
in 1926, was the first agricultural bank created in Mexico and for nine years
extended financial and technical assistance to both farmers and ejidatarios.
Since 1935, however, when the Banco Nacional de Credito Ejidal was formed to
take over all government banking and technical assistance activities in the
ejidal sub-sector, the Banco Agricola has served only farmers and ranchers
operating on privately-owned land, concentrating primarily on small farmers,
whether owners or tenants of their farms, who could not easily obtain credit
from private institutional sources. The main functions of Banco Agricola are
to:
(a) finance agricultural and livestock production activities;
(b) assist small farmers in organizing local credit societies
or 'grupos solidarios" through which they could not only
obtain more credit more easily but also purchase their
inputs and machinery and sell their products at better
prices; and
(c) provide assistance to its borrowers not only in connection
with production matters but also with regard to the processing
and marketing of farm output.
B. Organization and Management
2.        The Banco Agricola's policies and regulations are set by a Board
of Directors, headed by the Secretary of the Ministry of Agriculture and
Livestock and including in its members representatives of the Ministry of
Finance and Public Credit, the Banco de Mexico, and the lNational Association
of Small Farners. Operations are carried out under the direction of a
General Manager and an Assistant General Manager, assisted in their admini-
strative and planning duties by three recently established advisory units:
the Administrative Commission, the Organization and Methods Unit, and the
Programming Unit.
3.        The Banco Agricola system is composed of six Bancos Regionales
(wholly-owned subsidiaries of Banco Agricola and administered by a Board of
Directors, of which the General Manager of Banco Agricola is President) and
their agencies, the head offices and 25 branches of Banco Agricola, and a
few correspondent agencies. The following table shows, in terms of volume
of business transacted, the relative importance of the various members of
the Banco Agricola system as of October 31, 1972.

C. Lending Operations and Sources of Fund
Types of Loans
4.        The Banco Agricola lends to individual farmers, grupos solidarios
(three to nine members), and agricultural credit societies (ten or more
members) under the following categories:
(a) Production loans. These cover crop or livestock production
expenses and are secured primarily by a pledge of (a) the
iniputs or other materials purchased with the proceeds of
the loan, and (b) the resulting farm production. The amount
of such loans may not exceed 70% of the probable value of
farm products and they must be repaid with the income re-
sulting from the sale of these products.
(b)  Term loans.  These are for land clearing and plantations
and for the purchase of animals, tools, machinery and
equipment, and the like, secured by a mortgage of farm
assets and a pledge of the farm output to be produced over
the repayment period of the loan. Such loan may not exceed
the value of the assets to be acquired or of the improve-
ments to be financed with the loan proceeds and they may
not exceed 50% of the expected value of the farm output
during the loan repayment period. A repayment term of up
to 12 years may be granted.
(c) Commercial loans. Secured by agricultural or livestock
pre-ducts held in storage in warehouses designated by the
Bank, such loans may not exceed in amount 80% of the
value of the security pledged and are repayable in 180 days
or less.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
0
(d)  Real estate loans.  Covered under this category are loans
for permanent works, farm buildings, agro-industrial plants,
and such secured by a first mortgage on the assets financed
on other real estate. The amount of such loans may not
exceed 30% of the probable value of the farm output or of
the probable revenue of the borrower during the loan repay-
ment period. A repayment term of up to 20 years may be
granted.
Rates of Interest
5.        Until late 1972, the rates of interest on production loans varied
according to the purpose of the loan and the type of borrower, as follows:
Agricultural         Grupos
Credit Societies    Solidarios    Individuals
For basic crops   ..........   9.5               10.5             11.5
For other purposes   .......  10.5               11               12
For tern loans, the Bank has been charging interest rates of 10 and 10.5%
per annum on the unpaid balance, depending on the nature of the risk but
not on the type of borrower. Now, however, the interest rate is 7.6% to
low-income producers to conform with the legal rate established by the
Bank of Mexico for lending to such producers.
Volume of Business
6.        The volume of business carried out by the members of the Banco
Agricola system during the past four years was as follows:

the Banco's overall operations although it has guaranteed some term loans
made by private banks.

It can be seen that despite the Bank's efforts to encourage the formation of
credit groups, the relative importance of loans to individual farmers and
ranchers has grown from 62% to 69% of total loans in the period under review.
Overdue Loans
8.        Information on overdue loans is available only for the Banco
Agricola proper, exclusive of the Bancos Agrarios, and indicates, as shown
below, marked improvement in collections during the last three years.
Loan Portfolio at End of Year
In good standing       Overdue        % Overdue
(Millions of Mex$)
1968           422                 1,166           73.4
1969           462                 1,105           70.5
1970           665                 1,094           62.2
1971           722                1,244            63.3
9.        As in the case of Banco Ejidal, it is. alleged that the Government
has directed Banco Agricola from time to time to make loans funds available
for non-bankable programs aild this would account partly for the high volume
of overdues. However, there is evidence from recent anouncements that the
Government willput an end to this practice and the importance of such a move
could not be over-emphasized. On the other hand, the Government has asked
Banco Agricola to become considerably more active in term lending, a field
in which loan collection rates tend to be lower and one in which the Banco
has had little experience. Thus, it is crucial that this new course be
pursued very carefully which could be done under the guidance of the FONDO.
Sources of Funds
10.       The volume of lending of Banco Agricola is determined by it each
year in consultation with several Government ministries.  Estimates are



ANNEX 4
Page 5
based on analysis of farm credit plans and the availability of lending
resources but the figures must be approved by Government before it becomes
final. Seventy-five percent of the resources required by Banco Agricola
for short-term production loans are borrowed from the Banco Nacional de
Comercio Exterior and the balance is supplied as a grant from Government
as are all resources to support the Banco's limited term lending program.
Loan repayments, however, have generally not been more than sufficient to
meet its own debt maturities and, therefore, have not been available for
relending.
D. Technical and other Services to Small Farmers
11.       Technical assistance is provided largely by field inspectors
attached to its 116 agencies and zone offices but it is clear that the
Banco Agricola, which does a volume of business only one-half of that of
the Banco Ejidal's with only one-quarter of the staff, does not provide its
borrowers with the same degree of such help as does the latter. The main
duties of its field inspectors are, however, credit evaluation, disbursement,
and supervision and their next most important task is to promote the formation
of credit societies and "grupos solidarios," which leaves them little time
for technical assistance activities. The Banco realizes that, as more and
more credit groups are formed and as the volume of its term lending increases,
its capability to provide technical assistance will have to be better.  Not
only will the number of its field inspectors have to increase but their
qualifications will have to improve. With this in mind, the Banco is taking
a very active part in organizing a joint program for the training of Govern-
ment agricultural bank officers and field personnel, which is due to get
under way early in 1973.
Commercial Services
12.       The Banco Agricola assists small farmers who group themselves in
purchase-sale committees at the agency level in purchasing farm inputs and
equipment and in selling their farm products to best advantage. The volume
of commercial business carried out with such assistance has averaged Mex$63
million annually over the past four years and has yielded a small profit.
Agro-Industries
13.       The Banco Agricola has been reducing its agro-industrial activities
in the past few years and, while it still owns 16 units, 5 have been closed
down and 6 (all cattle-fattening yards) have been rented to a federal agency
called Industrial de Abastos. The volume of business done by Banco-owned
industrial units fell from Mex$49 million in 1968 to Mex$13 million in 1971
and net losses were also reduced during this period. Indications are that
the Banco will endeavor to cease industrial operations altogether as soon
as possible and to dispose of all its industrial assets.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
E. Operating Results and Financial Condition
14,       The large increase in banking income shown for 1971 in the state-
ments of Banco Agricola proper reflects an agreement reached with the Govern-
nent that assistance given to it towards the payment of interest on its obli-
gations would henceforth be treated as a subsidy and included in miscellaneous
income. lience, the Banco's 1971 and 1972 Statements of Income and Expenses
are not comparable with those of previous years.
15.       During the period 1968-72, interest collected on total loans out-
standing, including loans in good standing and loans in arrears, increased
from about 5% to about 6.2%. This is much lower than the average interest
rate of more than 10% which was charged by the Banco on its advances and
would indicate that interest is not being collected on a significant portion
of loans in arrears. Unlike the Banco Agricola proper, the Bancos Regionales
have had greater interest income than interest expense during this period,
reflecting the better quality of their loan portfolio.
16.       In absolute terms, there has been a moderate rise in total banking
expenses during the four-year period 1968-71 but general banking expenses
have decreased as a percentage of the volume of business done, both for
Banco Agricola and for Bancos Regionales. It is noteworthy that very small
charges have been made during this period for the provision for losses on
overdue accounts.
17.       Banco Agricola's financial condition has been greatly improved over
the past two years by substantial Government contributions which have helped
to reduce indebtedness to Banco Nacional de Comercio and to increase lending
resources. Even so, the Banco's financial situation is far less sound than
it appears from the balance sheet because of the failure to build up an
adequate reserve for possible losses on overdue loans. It is indeed a matter
of concern that its reserve for losses on past-due loans and other mis-
cellanous receivables corresponds to only 13% of the face value of these
assets (as compared to 69% in the case of Banco Ejidal) and that the reserve
for past-due loans, taken separately, is only about 5% of the value of such
loans.
F. Steps being taken to Improve the Banco's Performance
18.       As with Banco Ejidal, the main problers of Banco Agricola are
its low interest earnings and its unsatisfactory rate of loan collection. In
tthc past year, however, the Banco Agricola has made important strides in
streamlining its administrative, accounting, and data collection and processing
systems and procedures and has thus improved its overall efficiency, but
administrative reforms alone cannot solve all the problems which are closely
linked to Government agricultural credit policies. To improve the situation,



*                                                                          ANNEX 4
Page 7
the Government has, accordingly, decided to increase the flow of short- and
long-term credit funds to small farmers which should substantially increase
the volume of production loans of Banco Agricola over the next few years.
The Government has also just made available to the Banco, through the FONDO,
Mex$250 million with which to launch a major program of medium- and long-
term lending. The increase in the Banco's lending funds, coupled with the
involvement of the FONDO from the outset in setting up a term-lending program
for the Banco will force it to develop quickly the capability it needs to
operate soundly and effectively; this would have a salutary effect on the
Banco's collection problem.
19.       The Banco would also be required to provide more technical and
commercial assistance to its customers, but since this will more than absorb
the savings resulting from the administrative reforms being implemented,
operating costs will increase. This assistance should, however, increase
the debt servicing ability of customers and, in turn, have a favorable effect
on the loan collection rate.
20.       It is the Government's policy towards the Banco's involvement in
its credit programs, though, that is important to the Banco's performance in
the future. The Government has indicated that assistance to farmers for
purposes other than production increases would henceforth not be financed
from loan funds but from outright grants or subsidies.  Furthermore, it has
stressed that official agricultural banks should assess loan requests with
more care in order to improve the quality of loans and hence the rate of
loan collection. It remains to be seen how much leniency will be exercised
toward loan defaulters in the future, but this is clearly the single most
important determinant of Banco Agricola's future performance.
March 22, 1973

MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
PERFORMANCE UNDER FIRST, SECOND AND THIRD PROJECTS
A. Introduction
1.        The First, Second and Third Projects are livestock and agriculture
lending programs administered by FONDO 1/, as trustee for BANXICO. By agree-
ment with IBRD, all farming and ranching enterprises or processing industries
financed under the above Projects must have development plans that have had
prior evaluation by FONDO technicians (or FONDO-approved technicians) as to
their technical and financial feasibility. To assure effective use of bor-
rowed funds, these technicians also supervise the performance of sub-borrowers
to assure compliance with the approved development plans.
2.        Medium- and long-term loans to finance viable farm and agro-industrial
developments are disbursed by both private and public credit institutions par-
ticipating in the Projects who rediscount with the FONDO. Under the First
Project, rediscount funds came equally from the proceeds of Loan 430-ME
(US$25.0 million) and from the FONDO's own resources, generally borrowings
from BANXICO. 2/.  Under the Second and Third Projects, the arrangements are
the same except that the rediscount funds are 56.0% from the proceeds of
Loan 610-ME (US$65.0 million), and 747-ME (US$75.0 million), respectively,
and 44.0% from the FONDO's own resources.
B. Lending and Rediscounting
Rediscounting Policies and Procedures
3.        Under the First Project, participating banks (both official and
private credit institutions) rediscounted loans (regardless of loan size)
with FONDO at the following rates:
1/   See Annex 2 for a description of FONDO's powers, organization, and
other operations.
2/   Regulations require private banks in the Federal District to keep a
part of reserves against deposit liabilities in the form of "cattle
bonds" issued by the Government. Proceeds from these bonds are used
by BANXICO to lend to FONDO.



AN4NEX 5
Page 2                  l
% of Loan
Rediscounted with FONDO    Rediscount Rate       Interest Rate
(%)                (M
90                       7                 10
80                      6                  10
70                      5                  10
RA2AGROs and private banks rediscounted with FONDO, on average, about 71.1%
and 87.3%, respectively, of eligible loans.  Under the above rediscount rate
schedule, private banks generally obtained the highest return on their own
funds by rediscounting at the 90% level.
4.        One of the objectives of the Second Project was to further increase
fund participation by banks as compared with their contribution to the First
Project.  An attempt was made, therefore, under the Second Project to decrease
the average percentage of loans that may be rediscounted with FONDO; at the
same time, the revised rates were biased in favor of producers operating on
a small scale:

Agro-industry loan rediscounts were required to pay 1/2 of 1% more than the
above.
5.        In the Second Project, participating private banks and BANAGROs
rediscounted with FONDO 85.3% and 68.5%, respectively, of eligible loans.
Participating private banks increased their contribution by approximately
16%Z in the Second Project as contrasted with the First, due partly to the
changes in the rediscount rate schedule agreed between the IBRD and FONDO.
The elimination of 90% rediscounts for loans in excess of 'rex$500,000 was
probably the most important factor.
6.        One of the objectives of the Third Project was to still further
increase fund participation by banks as compared with their contributions
to the First and Second Projects. An attempt was made, therefore, under the
Third Project to decrease further the average percentage of loans that may
be rediscounted with FONDO by eliminating the option to rediscount 90% of

loans for all but very small banks, and by introducing changes in the re-

favor of small sub-loans:

/1   Available only to banks with paid-in capital of Mex$5 million or less.
The requirement that agro-industry loan rediscounts pay 1/2 of 1% more than
others (introduced in the Second Project) was dropped, because it was acting
as a deterrent to borrowing for desirable development.
7.        In the Third Project, participating private banks and BANAGROs
rediscounted with FONDO 80.1% and 76.3%, respectively, of eligible loans.
Participating private banks increased their contribution by approximately
35%0 in the Third Project as contrasted with the Second, due partly to the
changes in the rediscount rate schedule agreed between the Bank and FONDO.
The elimination of 90% rediscounts for all but the smallest banks was prob-
ably the most important factor (para 6).
Lending Terms and Conditions
8.        All loans to sub-borrowers in the First Project were at 10% per
annum. In some instances, however, private credit institutions also required
sub-borrowers to maintain a non-earning deposit account with the lending in-
stitutions equivalent to 10% to 20% of the loan.  This had the effect of
increasing interest cost to the sub-borrower by 1.1% to 2.5% per annum.
Every agro-industrial borrower had to pay a technical service fee of 1% of
the sub-loan amount in addition to these charges. Besides the regular in-
terest, some banks (especially "financieras") charged commissions of 1% of
the loan balance and inspection fees of Mex$200 to Mex$250.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
9.        In the Second Project, lending terms were somewhat modified.  Loans
of up to Mex$500,000 remained at 10% per annum, while all loans in excess of
this amount were at 11% per annum (para 4). The 1% technical service fee
charged to all agro-industrial sub-borrowers remained. By the time of the
appraisal of the Third Project in late 1970, no evidence was found that banks
any longer required a compulsory non-earning deposit (para 8).
10.       In the Third Project, lending terms were still further modified.
Loans of up to Mex$250,000 remained at 10% per annum, and loans in excess
of Mex$1,500,000 remained at 11% per annum, while loans between Mex$250,000
and Mex$1,500,000 were raised to 10.5% from 10% per annum. The requirement
under the First and Second Projects that all agro-industrial sub-borrowers
pay a 1% technical service fee was modified in the Third Project to effect
only loans of more than Mex$1,500,000.
11.       Generally, private credit institutions deal only with well-estab-
lished producers who are very creditworthy. Accordingly, while the develop-
ment period of a sub-loan might be much longer, actual terms of repayment
are shorter--generally from three to five years.
12.       The BANAGROs are prepared to deal with less well-established pro-
ducers, and offer better maturity terms and grace periods, therefore, meeting
a demand that private banks find it difficult to fill.

repayment proceeds. Total rediscounts granted by FONDO during the period
December 8, 1965 to March 31, 1969 amounted to Mex$868.6 million as follows:

15.       The decline in the percentage of eligible loans rediscounted with
FONDO from 85.5% in the First Project to 81.6% in the Second Project results
from two factors: (a) the changes introduced in the rediscount rates sched-
ule (para 4); and (b) the increased role of the BANAGROs (para 12). In the
Second Project, the BANAGROs rediscounted Mex$249.2 million, or 17.2%, of
the total rediscounted with FONDO, whereas under the First Project, the
BANAGROs rediscounted only 9.5% of the total.

17.       The further decline in the percentage of eligible loans rediscounted
with FONDO from 81.6% in the Second Project to 79.7 in the Third resulted from
the changes introduced in the rediscount rate schedule (para 6).
Participating Banks
18.       To date, participating banks have opened credit lines with the
FONDO which cumulatively total Mex$3,855.2 million (including First, Second
and Third Projects) against which they have rediscounted Mex$2,929.2 million.
FONDO has encouraged the opening of credit lines to promote the Project and
the participation of private banks in the development of agriculture. Under
the Third Project, there were 117 private credit institutions and six regional
BANAGROs participating as of September 30, 1972.



ANNEX 5
Page 6                   0
19.       Most of the private banks have fully used and renewed-their credit
lines. At the start of the Second Project, the Bank suggested that FONDO
periodically review with participating banks their use of credit lines, and
also that FONDO increase its promotional work, both at the level of participa-
ting banks and potential sub-borrowers. This, FONDO has done to the great
advantage of the Second and Third Projects.
Project Lending
20.       In the First Project, loan beneficiaries were commercial producers
operating in three principal regions: (a) tropics, (b) central plateau, and
(c) north Mexico.  The allocation of loan funds forecast by IBRD and the
actual distribution are quite comparable (see following table):

An important trend has been a diminishing average loan size. Whereas IBRD
in 1965 forecast an average loan size of about Mex$420,600, the actual average
loan size by June 30, 1968 was Mex$236,000. Furthermore, between June 30,
1968 and March 31, 1969, about 2,867 loans were granted with an average size
of only Mex$151,700. This is a dramatic decrease of 36% in average loan size
in six months. Under the Second Project, the average loan size dropped even
further to Mlex$133,800. Todate, under the Third Project, average loan size
is Mex$157,000.
21.       As of September 20, 1971, participating banks had granted MIex$1,774.4
million of loans under the Second Project, of which Mex$1,448.6 million had
been rediscounted with FONDO. The percentage distribution of loan funds fore-

22.       The Second Project was based on a three-year commitment period and
on a five-year disbursement. By September 20, 1971, the loan was fully dis-
bursed. Actual livestock lending was compatible with the forecasts, but
there was a much stronger demand for crop development loans (especially for
annual crops), while loans for agro-industries was less than expected. In-
creasing numbers of crop farmers were seeking loans under the Project as a
consequence of favorable lending conditions (for instance, suppliers' credit
is generally at 18% p.a. for no more than two years), and more banks were
using FONDO's rediscounting facilities for their clients as more technical
services become available. Inadequate organization of FONDO's Agro-industries
Division, which under the Third Project FONDO tried to remedy, also contributed
to the slow agro-industry lending.
23.       As of September 30, 1972, participating banks had granted Mex$866.5
million of loans under the Third Project, of which Mex$690.8 million had been
rediscounted with FONDO. The percentage distribution of loan funds forecast
by IBRD in autumn 1970, and the actual distribution to September 30, 1972 are
very similar (see following table):

24.       The Third Project was based on a two-year commitment period and a
four-year disbursement period.  Agro-industry lending continued to lag despite
efforts to improve the organization and functioning of the FONDO's Agro-
industries Division.
0



ANNEX 5
Page 8
25.       Livestock Development.  Of the total livestock lending under the
Third Project, beef cattle ranching received about 67%, dairying 19%, and
pigs and poultry 14%. About 68% of the investment in beef cattle ranching
and dairying was for the purchase of breeding animals, compared with an es-
timated 47% at appraisal. This reflects the situation in many areas where
most ranches, particularly the smaller ones in the wet tropics, are grossly
understocked and are not able to utilize their pasture properly.  In such
cases, it is uneconomic to invest extensively in on-ranch improvements unless
sufficient animals are available to fully utilize these investments.
26.       In the beef sub-sector half the loans and a third of the loan funds
went to ranchers with less than 300 ha.
27.       Crop Development.  About half the annual crop loans went to farmers
with less than 100 ha each, whereas about half the perennial crops went to
farmers with less than 50 ha each. Annual crop loans were concentrated in
the west, northwest and northeast, and perennial crop loans were mostly made
in the west and northeast.
28.       The most important inputs financed by annual crop loans were:
(a) farm machinery and equipment, 36%; and (b) on-farm irrigation works, 13%.
Perennial crops loans financed mostly establishment and maintenance of crops,
58%; farm machinery and equipment, 15%; and on-farm irrigation works, 13%.
29.       Agro-industries Development.  In the First Project, FONDO made ap-
proximately 319 loans for agro-industries, slightly less than 50% for live-
stock processing industries, and the remainder for crop storage and processing.
Investments in the tropics for agro-processing industries were minimal. Under
the Second Project, there were about 56 loans for agro-industries, and under
the Third Project (up to September 30, 1972), there were about 35 loans for
agro-industries.
C. Project Performance and Impact
Performance
30.       The project has succeeded in widening the role of the banking system
in technically-supervised lending to farm development. The technical services
of FONDO and participating banks have improved throughout the lives of the
three IBRD Projects. Adjustments to the organization of FONDO, made in 1969
at the request of IBRD, have been successful in strengthening FONDO technical
staff work. Adequate ranch and farm plans are now being prepared and super-
vision of investments has improved considerably.  There are still, however,
insufficient supervision and technical services, especially after the on-farm
investments are made; consequently, insufficient technical advice and guidance
are being given the individual sub-borrower, especially in livestock. This
has limited the Project's ability to introduce new technology to many producers.



ANNEX 5
Page 9
31.       Evaluations in agro-industries are generally satisfactory though
certain weaknesses exist in the ability of FONDO staff to manage this field
of activity. The Agro-industries Division of FONDO needs strengthening in
its leadership. FONDO also needs to develop a marketing capability at least
sufficient to enable it to evaluate marketing studies done by other individuals
or organizations.
Results of Operations and Financial Conditions of FONDO
32.       The Special Fund created for IBRD Projects is in good financial
condition. All claims are against sound financial institutions.
33.       Aside from the IBRD Projects, FONDO is also trustee for other
lending programs. The results of FONDO's entire operations are fully dis-
cussed in Annex 2.
Impact of Lending
34.       As agreed under the Second and Third Project Agreements, FONDO has
taken steps to measure development impact on a systematic formal basis. Early
in 1972 FONDO hired a consultant to help them in measuring impact. The results
reported here are based upon two distinct field surveys conducted, under the
supervision of the consultant, during May 1972.
35.       A total of 18,043 sub-borrower loans were approved for rediscounting
under the First and Second Projects. Of this total, 17,811 loans were made to
farms and ranches, and 232 loans were made to agro-industries. Recognizing
the smaller number, and distinctly different character, and average size of
the agro-industry loans, the consultant decided to sample this group separately.
The target sample size he chose was 4% of the farms and ranches, and 25% of
the agro-industries. A total of 721 farm and ranch sub-borrowers, and 54
agro-industries sub-borrowers were selected at random. Questionnaires were
designed, pre-tested and adapted to obtain, on the basis of a single interview,
data on the financial, and production coefficients prevailing before develop-
ment and in calendar year 1971.
36.       The analysis consistently supports the general hypothesis that
borrowers, considered in total, have benefitted greatly from these projects.
No attempt was made to establish a clear cause-effect relationship between
the loan investments facilitated by the credit projects and borrower progress.
The amount of sub-borrower progress achieved to date was measured in several
ways. The basic procedure employed was to compare the current situation
(calendar year 1971) against that which held prior to the initial FONDO proj-
ect loan in each sample case. As should be expected, the amount of progress
varied greatly between individual survey respondents. On the average, however,
all developmental progress measures applicable to the complete business
entity were found to be highly impressive.
0



ANNEX 5
Page 10
37.       There was substantial average size variation between the various
types of enterprises and within the different enterprises. Overall, the
average size of farm/ranch was 552 ha, ranging from an average of 1,000 ha
for beef ranches to 85 ha in the case of pig producers. The average size
of farm producing annual crops was 394 ha and ranged from 614 ha in arid and
semi-arid areas to 300 in the humid tropics. It should be emphasized, how-
ever, that area of farm is a very poor measure of size of operation in Mexico
due to great variability in water availability and soil quality.
38.       The average individual loan size on the sample farms/ranches was
US$13,600, but since about 27% received repeat loans, the average borrowing
per farm or ranch was US$18,350. Average borrowing per farm/ranch ranged
from about US$40,000 for poultry sub-projects, to US$13,700 for beef sub-
projects.
39.       The contribution of farm/ranch sub-borrowers to total investment
averaged 15%. This is in line with IBRD expectations at time of appraisal.
Perennial crop sub-borrowers on average only contributed 8% of investment
cost, whereas pig and beef sub-borrowers contributed on average 2t% of
investment costs. The reason for the difference is not readily apparent.
40.       The breakdown of investment expenditures on the sample farms/ranches
was as follows: land improvement, 19%; irrigation, 5%; machinery and equip-
ment purchases, 25%o; buildings, 15%; and livestock for breeding purposes, 36..
41.       On the average sample farm/ranch, the gross value of farm production
almost doubled between pre-development and calendar year 1971, while net cash
operating income per farm increased by 45%. The greatest improvement in net
cash operating income was on the beef and dairy farms (doubling), and the
least on perennial crop farms (8%).
42.       The sample survey also tried to compare production coefficient
oeiore development and in calendar year 1971.  For this purpose, a few key
coefficients were selected. On beef farms, the average herd size increased
fron 98 cows to 134 cows, but, the weaning percentage did not change signi-
2cantl,.   cokkever, stocking rate improved about 10o.  In addition, the aver-
age annual weight gairt iu calves was about 8% higher in calendar year 1971
1:han before development.  These improvemants in production coefficients are
.onsistent with the FONDO emphasis on improving grazing conditions.  Nowevex,
the failure to raise weaning rates is disappointing. In the case for crops,
-t as generally found that production coefficients did not change appreciably.
43,       The survey of agro-industries involved a sample of 54 firms, from
the total of 205 such firms who received FONDO loans under the First and
Second Projects. The survey included respondents from 13 different industrie6,
so generalizations are very difficult. Nevertheless, there are some interest-
ing findings.  Only 30% of the sample firms were in existence prior to the
initial project loan, and some 39% of the sample were cooperative type under-
taking.  About 26% of the firms had multiple loans.  On average, sub-borrowers
0



ANNEX 5
Page 11
contributed 40% of investment cost for their undertaking. An average invest-
ment was US$235,000 distributed as follows: land, 3%; buildings, 31%; machinery
and equipment, 64%; and other, 2%.  A rather surprising finding is that on
average 9 new jobs were created per firm, at a cost of US$26,000 per job.
Financial indicators were very satisfactory for the group as a whole.
44.       This study by no means captures the ultimate, potential impact of
the credit projects upon sub-borrowers. Respondents were selected at random,
regardless of the date of their initial project loan. Investments of the
type involved here require years to come into full fruition.

ANNEX 11
MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PRoJECT
THE OUTLOOK FOR COTTON
A. The World. Outlook 1/
'.         Cotton is produced in varying quantities in more than 80 countries.
Roughly one-third. of the world, crop is produced for export in raw (fiber)
form and significant amounts are exported by producing countries as textiles.
The United States, the USSR and Mainland. China are the world's three largest
producers, accounting together for about dne-half of the world. output. The
bulk of the remainder is produced. by a large number of developing countries.
2.         The United. States remains the world's leading cotton exporter,
although its share in the world. cotton trade has been declining historically.
During the past ten years U.S. exports accounted on the average for 22 per-
cent of the world's total exports. Over the same period, centrally planned.
countries accounted for another 13 percent and developing countries for the
remaining 65 percent (Appendix 11-1).
3.         The present world, cotton situation is in many respects different
from what is was throughout most of the past and. especially the early and.
mid-sixties. Since 1966-67 world, cotton consumption has been rising by only
0.9 percent a year compared to the annual growth rate of about 2.2 percent
experienced. in the preceding 11 years. On the other hand., production, which
had. increased by 2.1 percent a year during the period between 1955-56 and.
1965-66, declined sharply in 1966/67 and 1967/68 and remained below consunp-
tion for five consecutive years.
4.         These changes in the demand-supply relationship which took place
in the late sixties changed c5siderably the world. cotton position.  From
a peak of 30.7 million bales - at the beginning of the 1966 season (August)
- equivalent to 7.2 months of world. consumption - stocks declined to 19.0
million bales by August 1972, or to the equivalent of little more than four
months' consumption, a leyel considered as below minimum normal requirements
by consuming countries..2' The distribution of stocks also changed. consid-
erably: the United. States held in August 1971 23 percent of world. stocks
(compared. to 55 percent in August 1966), centrally planned. countries 18 per-
cent (11.4 percent in 1966) and the rest of the world. 59 percent (33.6 per-
cent in 1966). Reflecting these changes in the demand.-supply balance, the
Liverpool price of Mexican Strict Middling 1-1/161" cotton - a representative
1/ Prepared. by the Economic Analysis and Projections Department, IBRD.
2/ One bale contains 478 pounds of cotton.
3/ The consensus of consuming countries on a normal level of commercial
stocks is that they should. be sufficient to cover 5-6 months of world.
consumption.



ANNEX I1
Page 2
grade of so-called medium-long cotton - which was 28.1 cents per pound in
August 1966 rose to 31.0 cents per pound. in August 1970 and continued to
increase throughout most of the 1970/71 season to reach the peak of 40-41
cents per pound in January-February 1972.
5.         A strong supply response to the high prices which prevailed during
the 1970/71 season, however, materialized in the following season and almost
as rapidly as they rose in the preceding 9-10 months, cotton prices began to
decline in the Spring and. Summer of 1972. During the current cotton season
(1972/73), moreover, world cotton supply is forecast to be abindant (at 2-3
A,'lion bales above expected total consumption). This expected excess
supply wi1l considerably ease the world stock position and bring again
cotton stocks to more desirable levels (about 23 million bales).
6.         World cotton prices in current terms are expected to decline by
some 15 percent from recent high levels (1969/70-1971/721/) by the mid-
seventies.  Tn terms of Mexican SM 1-1/16", a representative grad.e of medium-
long cotton which normally accounts for more than 60 percent of total world
cotton output, this means a price of about 28a0 U.S. cents per pound, c.i.f.
Liverpool.  By the end of the seventies, c.i.f. Liverpool cotton prices, in
current terms, are forecast to be only marginally above l97 /,6 levels at
some 29.0 U.S. cents per pound. In 1972 U.S. dollar terms, /2 Mexican SK
1-1/16" cotton prices are expected tu be at about 26.0 cents per pound by
the mid.-seventies and at 25.b cents per pound by 1980.
7.         The expected downward trend in price over the medium-term reflects
a number of assamptions:  (a) that the current high prices are tenporary and
reflect a demand-supply imbalance caused largely by a temporary supply short-
age; (b) that in the tbsence of a decline in prices production will tend
again to outpace consuLption in the mid-seventies;3/ (c) that inter-fiber
competition will continue to limit the growth of cotton consumption but that
no major breakthrough in man-made fiber technology will greatly accelerate
recent substitution trends; and (d) that price adjustments will take place
within the framework of a relatively f1reer, more competitive market. This
in turn implies continued competition among some sixty exporting countries
(mary of them heavly dependent on cotton exports); absence, in future U.S.
price support and cotton export policies, of the elements which made that
country a residual supplier in the world market and. led to recurring stock
accumLlation in the United. States; and, finally no international cotton
agreement.
1/ See Appendix 11-2.
2/ The purchasing power of the U. S. dollar is assumed to decline by 2.0
percent per annum during the seventies.
3/ Available information on government plans or intentions (as supplied to
the International Cotton Advisory Committee in October 1972) indicate
that world cotton production could. exceed. demand in the mid-seventies
by some 5-7 percent.



ANNEX 31
Page 3
8.         The future growth of cotton production depends not only on the
natural supply response to change in price but also - and quite crucially -
on the cotton policies of the major producers. The United States and.the
USSR account together for almost 39 percent of world cotton production.
Their production policies necessarily influence the growth path of world
cotton supply. In our supply projections we have assumed. a 'normaliza-
tion" of U.S. cotton output by 1975 to about 13 million bales (in line with
U.S. stated intentions and current policies) and total output of about 11
million bales in the Soviet Union (again in line with its expansion plans).
The combined output share of the two countries would therefore remain the
same as during the past five seasons. Should the U.S. cotton output remain
around. the 10-11 bales of the past three seasons and the USSR output stabilize
at 10 million bales, the 1975 projected surplus situation would turn, other
things being equal, into one of rough demand-supply equilibrium and lead to
actual prices 5 percent higher than those forecast in paragraph 4,
9.         Demand uncertainties, however, call for a certain degree of pru-
dence in forecasting cotton prices. World cotton consumption is projected
to grow at an average annual rate of 1.6 percent during the next ten years.
Even this modest growth rate of cotton eonsunption presupposes some further
improvement in the quality composition of cotton and progress in promotional
activity, and furthermore, that no new breakthrough in the production of man-
made fibers would occur - and lead to a faster substitution of cotton with
man-made (especially synthetic) fibers. As mentioned before, cotton consump-
tion did. in fact slow down considerably in the late sixties and. grew at a very
modest average aanual rate of 0.9 percent between 1966/67 and 1971/72. The
declining trend in cotton utilization in developed countries since the mid-
sixties - largely due to greater competition from man-mad.e (especially non-
cellulosic) fibers - was less than fullyooupenutei for by increased consump-
tion in developing and centrally planned countries. Competition from synthetic
substitutes, although particularly strong in industrialized countries, is by
no means restricted to these markets, In Central America, South America and
South Asia synthetic fibers have already penetrated the market and further
inroads are expected. during the next decade. During the past ten years, while
cotton's share of world total fiber market fell from nearly 66 to 44 percent,
the market share of man-made fibers increased from 29 to 50 percent.
10.        Inter-fiber competition hinges upon two main factors:  relative
prices and product performance. Prices of man-mad.e fibers (especially poly-
ester fibers) declined considerably in the past five years thereby moving
price parities in favor of man-made fibers. Recent high cotton prices, more-
over, are likely to accelerate the trend in substitution of cotton with poly-
ester fibers, particularly in developed countries. The quality or product
performance of man-made fibers is superior to cotton in some crucial end-uses
(e.g., apparel) where cotton lacks, the "easy-care" properties characteristic
of synthetic fibers (alone or blended.with cotton). Although further sharp
declines in the prices of the most important non-cellulosic fibers do not
appear very likely in the short ran, the possibility of increased competition
from these fibers still remains quite strong, given the technical advrantages
that man-made fibers have in new high-speed spinning and. weaving machines.
This could seriously affect cotton demand and slow down its growth to a very
modest 1 percent per annum over the next ten years.



ANNEX 11
Page 4
11.        At projected equilibrium levels of demand, supply and price for
the mid-seventies, world raw cotton exports would epVerience a growth rate of
around 1.2 percent per annum between 1967-69 and 1975, although developing
countries' exports may grow at a higher rate (between 1.5 and 2.0 percent per
annum). Assuming that world cotton prices move in the late seventies toward
long-term equilibrium levels, world raw cotton exports may continue to grow
throughout the late seventies at approximately the same rate envisaged for
the earlier period.  Export shares, however, become more difficult to predict,
given their dependence on possible future policy actions by major producing
and exporting countries such as the United States and the USSR.
12.        The export performance and prospects of individual developing
countries will continue to vary depending, inter alia, on availability of land,
trends in yields, quality ed marketability of cotton grown, domestic consump-
tion trends and. cotton textiles trade balances. Competition among producing
countries is expected to continue and small producing countries with limited
production alternatives are likely to push output and succeed in expanding
their small shares. The world market growth, however, is expected to be a
slow one throughout the seventies.  Competing man-made fibers will continue
to exert pressure on cotton and to limit its growth.  The balance hetween
advancements in cotton agronozy and man-made fiber technology, although
difficult to predict, is likely to remain tilted in favor of competing man-
made fibers.
Miarch 21, 1973

ANNEX 13
MEXICO
FOURTH LIVESTOCK AND AGRICEJITURE DEVELOPNENT PROJECT
Economic Rate of Return
1.         The economic rates of return of the Sub-Projects, General and
Low-Income Producers, were calculated on the basis of data shown in
Appendixes 13-1 and. 13-2. Key assumptions and adjustments made were that:
(a) the prices of investment and operating cost items, in real
terms, would remain at current price levels;
(b) the prices of all outputs, in real terms, would remain at
current price levels, with the exception of cotton prices,
which are assumed to decline by 15 in the 1970ts and remain
at that level thereafter for the life of the Project (these
assumptions are based.on commodity price projections);
(c) incremental herd values were credited to the Project at the
end of the life of the investments;
(d) the official rate of exchange is equal to the shadow rate of
exchange;
(e)  all FONDO administrative and technical services provided. to
the Project were charged to the Project;
(f) the cost of banking services provided to the Project by
participating private and public banks were estimated and.
charged to the Project;
(g) taxes, which would, be transfer payments, were not charged
to the Project;
(h) the opportunity cost of labor employed on the General Sub-
Project is equal to the monetary wage because most of the
workers are semi-skilled agricultural laborers; and
(i) the opportunity cost of labor eployed on the Low-Income
Producerst Sub-Project, on average, is 25% of the monetary
wage level.
2.         Based on these assumptions, the economic rate of return of the
Project is estimated at approximately 29%, having incorporated all signifi-
cant adjustments to producer benefits that were necessary to better reflect
real costs and returns to the economy. The economic rate of return on the
General Sub-Project and the Low-Income Producerst Sub-Project are estimated
at 26% and. 41a, respectively.  If labor on the Low-Income Producers' Sub-
Project were charged at the monetary wage, then the return on this Sub-



AMNEX 13
P age 2 
Project would drop to 25%. Assuming further that the agro-indhstries
component of the Project remains unchanged, but that actual incremental
production from the other components is valued at JC% less, due either to
a drop in all output prices or a short-fall in prodaction,then the economic
rates of return on the General Sub-Project and the Low-Income Prodboers'
Sub-Project would be 21% and 3 %, respectively. Should investment costs
be 1% higher tban projected, then the economic rate of return on the
Project would be about 27%.
.
April 19, 1973
